```
                                                                                                                                                          H_{j.8} ,H_{j.9}

\mathcal{PG} \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathsf{PGGen}(1^{\lambda}), \vec{a}, \vec{b} \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathsf{DDH}, \mathbf{U} \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathbb{Z}_{p_{-}}^{2 \times d}, (n, |\mathsf{ct}|, |\mathsf{sk}|) \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathsf{Param}(\mathcal{P}), \text{ for all } i \in [n], \mathbf{W}_i \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{2 \times 2}
\vec{a}^{\perp}, \vec{b}^{\perp} \leftarrow_{\mathbf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2 \setminus \{\mathbf{0}\} \text{ s.t.} \vec{a}^{\top} \vec{a}^{\perp} = \vec{b}^{\top} \vec{b}^{\perp} = 0, \forall i \in [n] : v_i \leftarrow_{\mathbf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p
\mathsf{mpk} := \left( [\vec{a}]_1, [\vec{b}]_2, [\mathbf{U}^\top \vec{a}]_1, \{ [\mathbf{W}_i^\top \vec{a}]_1, [\mathbf{W}_i \vec{b}]_2 \}_{i \in [n]} \right)

\mathsf{ct}^{\star} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{OEnc}(\mathsf{P}^{\star}, \vec{x}^{\star}) \\ b \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{OKeyGen}(\cdot)}(\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{ct}^{\star})
 \mathsf{OEnc}(\mathsf{P}^\star, \vec{x}^\star):
 \overline{[\vec{c}_1]_1} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{G}_1^2, \mathbf{C} := \mathsf{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}), [\mathbf{C}_2]_1 := [(\mathbf{W}_1^\top \vec{c}_1| \cdots | \mathbf{W}_n^\top \vec{c}_1) \mathbf{C}]_1

   [\mathbf{C}_2]_1 = [(\mathbf{W}_1^{\top} \vec{c}_1 | \cdots | \mathbf{W}_n^{\top} \vec{c}_1) \mathbf{C}]_1
 \overline{[\vec{c}_3]_1 := [\vec{x}^\star + \mathbf{U}^\top \vec{c}_1]_1}.
 Return (P^*, [\vec{c}_1]_1, [\mathbf{C}_2]_1, [\vec{c}_3]_1)
 OKeyGen(att, \vec{y}):
 On the the \rho-th query:
 r \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p, [\vec{k}_1]_2 := [\vec{b}r]_1, \mathbf{K} := \mathsf{EncKey}(\mathsf{att}).
 If \rho = j + 1 and \mathsf{P}^{\star}(\mathsf{att}) = 0, then [\vec{k}_1]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{G}_2^2.
 If \rho=j+1 and \mathsf{P}^{\star}(\mathsf{att})=0, then [\vec{k}_1]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathbb{G}_2^2 \setminus \mathsf{span}([\vec{b}]_2)
 If \rho \leq j and \mathsf{P}^{\star}(\mathsf{att}) = 0, then: [\mathbf{K}_2]_2 := [(\widetilde{\mathbf{U}}\vec{y}|\mathbf{W}_1\vec{k}_1|\cdots|\mathbf{W}_n\vec{k}_1)\mathbf{K}]_2
If \rho > j or \mathsf{P}^\star(\mathsf{att}) = 1, then: [\mathbf{K}_2]_2 := [(\mathbf{U} \vec{y} | \mathbf{W}_1 \vec{k}_1 | \cdots | \mathbf{W}_n \vec{k}_1) \mathbf{K}]_2
 If \rho = j + 1 and P^*(\mathsf{att}) = 0, then:
 \vec{u} = \mathbf{U} \vec{y}, \, \alpha = 0, \, \stackrel{\ldots}{\alpha} \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p \, , \, \, \vec{u} = \widetilde{\mathbf{U}} \vec{y} \, ,
    [\mathbf{K}_2]_2 := [\vec{u}|\mathbf{W}_1\vec{k}_1|\cdots|\mathbf{W}_n\vec{k}_1)\mathbf{K} + (\vec{a}^\perp(\vec{b}^\perp)^\top\vec{k}_1)(\alpha|v_1|\dots|v_n)\mathbf{K}]_2
   if \rho=j+1 and \mathsf{P}^\star(\mathsf{att})=0, then:[\mathbf{K}_2]_2:=[(\widetilde{\mathbf{U}}\vec{y}|\mathbf{W}_1\vec{k}_1|\cdots|\mathbf{W}_n\vec{k}_1)\mathbf{K}]_2
Return (att, \vec{y}, [\vec{k}_1]_2, [\mathbf{K}_2]_2)
```

<span id="page-15-0"></span>**Fig. 5.** Hybrid games for the proof of Lemma 3.4, where  $H_j$  is defined for all  $j \in [Q]$ , and  $H_{j,i}$  are defined for all  $j \in [Q-1]$ ,  $i \in \{1...9\}$ . The six type of boxes denote which figure components belong to the hybrid.

**Game** Ideal $_{\mathcal{A},\mathcal{S}}^{\mathcal{F}\mathcal{E}}(1^{\lambda})$ : we show this game is statistically close to  $\mathsf{G}_2$ . The simulator  $\mathcal{S}:=(\mathsf{Setup},\mathsf{Enc},\mathsf{KeyGen})$  is described in Fig. 6. First, we use the fact that for all  $\vec{a}\in\mathbb{Z}_p^2$ , the following distributions are within 1/p statistical distance:

$$\vec{c}_1 \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2 \text{ and } \vec{c}_1 \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2 \setminus \mathsf{span}(\vec{a})$$

The leftmost distribution corresponds to  $G_2$ , whereas the rightmost distribution corresponds to  $\operatorname{Ideal}_{\mathcal{A},\mathcal{S}}^{\mathcal{F}\mathcal{E}}(1^{\lambda})$ . Then, we use the fact that for all  $\vec{x}^{\star} \in \mathbb{Z}^d$ , the following distributions are identical:

$$(\vec{a}, \vec{c}_1, \widetilde{\mathbf{U}}, \mathbf{U})$$
 and  $(\vec{a}, \vec{c}_1, \widetilde{\mathbf{U}}, \mathbf{U} - \vec{a}^{\perp} (\vec{x}^{\star})^{\top}),$ 

where  $\vec{a} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{DDH}$ ,  $\vec{c}_1 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2 \setminus \mathsf{span}(\vec{a})$ ,  $\mathbf{U} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{2 \times d}$ ,  $\vec{u}_0 := \frac{\mathbf{U}^\top \vec{a}}{\|\vec{a}\|_2^2}$ ,  $\widetilde{\mathbf{U}} := \vec{a} \vec{u}_0^\top$ , and  $\vec{a}^\perp \in \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  such that  $\vec{a}^\top \vec{a}^\perp = 0$  and  $\vec{c}_1^\top \vec{a}^\perp = 1$ . This is because  $\mathbf{U}$  is a uniformly random matrix, so adding an offset  $-\vec{a}^\perp (\vec{x}^\star)^\top$  does not change

{16}------------------------------------------------

its distribution. This extra offset doesn't appear in  $\widetilde{\mathbf{U}}$  since  $\vec{a}^{\top}\vec{a}^{\perp}=0$ . The leftmost distribution corresponds to  $\mathsf{G}_2$ , whereas the rightmost distribution corresponds to  $\mathsf{Ideal}_{\mathcal{A},\mathcal{S}}^{\mathcal{F}\mathcal{E}}(1^{\lambda})$ . Putting everything together, we obtain:

$$|\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{G}_2}(\mathcal{A}) - \Pr[1 \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{Ideal}_{\mathcal{A},\mathcal{S}}^{\mathcal{F}\mathcal{E}}(1^{\lambda})]| \leq \frac{1}{n}.$$

\operatorname{\mathsf{and}} \vec{a}^{\top} \vec{a}^{\perp} = 0, \\ \mathbf{U} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{2 \times d}, \vec{u}_0 := \frac{\mathbf{U}^{\top} \vec{a}}{\|\vec{a}\|_2^2}, (\operatorname{\mathsf{att}}, \vec{y}, [\vec{k}_1]_2, [\vec{k}_2]_2) \end{aligned}

<span id="page-16-0"></span>Fig. 6. PPT simulator for the security proof of the FE scheme from Fig. 4.

{17}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-17-2"></span><span id="page-17-0"></span>3.2 FE with adaptive, indistinguishability based security

In this section, we build FE schemes for the family of functions  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d,B),\mathcal{P}}$ , where  $\mathcal{P}$  corresponds to identity-based encryption, inner-product predicate encryption, or even monotone span programs. Similarly to the selective construction in Section 3.1, we give a modular construction that builds upon a simple, information-theoretic, one-time secure object, that generalizes the notion of predicate encoding to functions, hence called function encoding. Namely, a function encoding is a private-key version of functional encryption that only satisfies a one-time security notion.

Recall that our construction from Section 3.1 fails to achieve adaptive security, even if the underlying building blocks are adaptively secure. The reason is that, throughout the security proof, only the functional decryption keys associated with a pair (att,  $\vec{y}$ ) such that  $P^*(att) = 0$  can be turned to semi-functional, where  $P^*$  is the predicate chosen by the adversary for the challenge ciphertext. In fact, the other keys cannot be turned semi-functional, since they must decrypt correctly the challenge ciphertext, and not just ciphertexts that can be generated from the public key. This challenge does not arise in the typical dual system encryption methodology used for ABE, since none of the queried keys can decrypt.

A similar situation arose in the context of fully-hiding predicate encryption for inner products, where ciphertexts are associated with a vector  $\tilde{\vec{x}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$ , functional decryption keys are associated with  $\tilde{\vec{y}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$ , and decryption successfully recovers the plaintext if  $\tilde{\vec{x}}^{\top}\tilde{\vec{y}}=0$ , whereas no information about that plaintext is revealed otherwise. As opposed to regular inner-product encryption, the vector  $\tilde{\vec{x}}$  is also hidden, the only bit of information that leaks is whether  $\vec{x}^{\top}\vec{y} = 0$  or not. In this context, the adversary can query functional decryption keys that decrypt the challenge ciphertext. This is still a meaningful security notion since  $\vec{x}$  remains hidden even when such keys are queried.

We show that the techniques introduced by [OT12], later improved in [CGW18] for adaptively secure fully-hiding predicate encryption for inner products are also relevant to obtain adaptively secure inner-product FE with fine-grained access control (even when the predicate is not hidden). In fact, using function encodings, a new notion we introduce that subsumes the notion of predicate encoding introduced in [Att14, Wee14] in the context of adaptively-secure ABE, we generalize the approach of [OT12,CGW18] to a large class of functional encryption schemes, whereas their scheme corresponds to the special case of inner-product encryption. Namely, we compile any function encoding for the function family  $\mathcal{F}$  into an adaptively secure FE for the same class of functions from the SXDH assumption in asymmetric pairings. In Appendix C, we give concrete function encodings that correspond to identity-based encryption, innerproduct predicate encryption, fully-hiding inner-product predicate encryption and monotone span programs.

<span id="page-17-1"></span>**Definition 3.5** (function encoding). Let  $\mathcal{F}$  be a family of functions where each function  $f \in \mathcal{F}$  is of the form  $f: \mathcal{X} \to \mathbb{Z}_p$ , and p be a prime. A function encoding for  $(\mathcal{F}, \mathbb{Z}_p)$  is given by the following polynomial-time deterministic algorithms:

- Param( $\mathcal{F}$ ): takes as input the family of functions  $\mathcal{F}$ , and returns the parameters  $(n, |\mathsf{ct}|, |\mathsf{sk}|) \in \mathbb{N}^3$ .
- $\mathsf{EncCt}(x)$ : takes as input  $x \in \mathcal{X}$ , and returns a matrix  $\mathbf{C} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+1) \times \mathsf{ct}}$ .
- EncKey(f): takes as input a function  $f \in \mathcal{F}$ , and returns a matrix  $\mathbf{K} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+1) \times |\mathsf{sk}|}$ . Decode $(f, \mathsf{part}(x))$ : takes as input the partial information  $\mathsf{part}(x)$  of  $x \in \mathcal{X}$  and  $f \in \mathcal{F}$ . It returns a vector  $\vec{d} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{|\mathsf{ct}|+|\mathsf{sk}|}$ . (See Section 2.2 for a discussion on the partial information).

We require the following properties.

**Correctness.** For all  $x \in \mathcal{X}$  and  $f \in \mathcal{F}$ ,  $\mathbf{C} := \mathsf{EncCt}(x) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+1) \times |\mathsf{ct}|}$ ,  $\mathbf{K} := \mathsf{EncKey}(f) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+1) \times |\mathsf{sk}|}$ ,  $\vec{d} := \mathsf{EncKey}(f) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+1) \times |\mathsf{ct}|}$ Decode $(f, \mathsf{part}(x))$ , we have:  $(\mathbf{C}|\mathbf{K})\vec{d} = (f(x), 0, \dots, 0) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n+1}$ . **Security.** For any  $x^0, x^1 \in \mathcal{X}$  and  $f \in \mathcal{F}$  such that  $f(x^0) = f(x^1)$  and  $\mathsf{part}(x^0) = \mathsf{part}(x^1)$ , the following are identically distributed:

$$\begin{split} \vec{v}^\top(\mathbf{C}|\mathbf{K}) \textit{ with } \mathbf{C} &:= \mathsf{EncCt}(x^0), \mathbf{K} := \mathsf{EncKey}(f) \\ \textit{ and } \\ \vec{v}^\top(\mathbf{C}|\mathbf{K}) \textit{ with } \mathbf{C} &:= \mathsf{EncCt}(x^1), \mathbf{K} := \mathsf{EncKey}(f), \end{split}$$

where  $\vec{v} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{n+1}$ .

{18}------------------------------------------------

**Example: Identity-Based Encryption.** Each function is described by an identity  $id \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  and a vector  $\vec{y} \in [0, B]^d$ , takes as input another identity  $id' \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  and a vector  $\vec{x} \in [0, B]^d$ , and outputs  $\vec{x}^\top \vec{y}$  if id = id', 0 otherwise. The partial information  $part(\vec{x}, id) = id$ .

- Param: returns the parameters (2d, |ct| = d, |sk| = n + 1).
- $\operatorname{EncCt}(\vec{x},\operatorname{id})$ : given  $\vec{x}\in\mathbb{Z}_p^n$  and  $\operatorname{id}\in\mathbb{Z}_p$ , returns a matrix  $\mathbf{C}\in\mathbb{Z}_p^{(2d+1)\times d}$  such that  $\mathbf{C}^{\top}(w_0,\vec{w}_1,\vec{w}_2)=(w_0\vec{x}+\vec{w}_1+\operatorname{id}\vec{w}_2)\in\mathbb{Z}_p^d$ .
- EncKey $(\vec{y}, \mathsf{id}')$ : given  $\vec{y} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  and  $\mathsf{id}' \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ , returns a matrix  $\mathbf{K} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(2d+1)\times 1}$  such that  $\mathbf{K}^\top(w_0, \vec{w}_1, \vec{w}_2) = \vec{y}^\top(\vec{w}_1 + \mathsf{id}'\vec{w}_2) \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ .
- Decode(id, id',  $\vec{y}$ ): if  $\vec{x}^{\top}\vec{y} = 0$ , it returns the vector  $\vec{d} := (\vec{y}, -1) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{d+1}$ .

Our modular construction is presented in Fig. 7. Proofs of correctness and security are given below.

```
\begin{split} & \underline{\mathsf{Setup}}(1^{\lambda},\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d,B),\mathcal{P}}) \colon \\ & \mathcal{PG} = (\mathbb{G}_1,\mathbb{G}_2,\mathbb{G}_T,p,P_1,P_2,e) \leftarrow \mathsf{PGGen}(1^{\lambda}), \vec{a} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{DDH}, \vec{b} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3, (n,|\mathsf{ct}|,|\mathsf{sk}|) \leftarrow \mathsf{Param}(\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d,B),\mathcal{P}}), \mathsf{for} \; \mathsf{all} \; i \in [0,n], \\ & \mathbf{W}_i \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{2\times3}, \mathsf{mpk} := \left([\vec{a}]_1, \{[\mathbf{W}_i^\top \vec{a}]_1\}_{i \in [n]}\right), \mathsf{msk} := \left([\vec{b}]_2, \{[\mathbf{W}_i \vec{b}]_2\}_{i \in [n]}\right). \; \mathsf{Return} \; (\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{msk}) \\ & \underline{\mathsf{Enc}}(\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{P}, \vec{x}) \colon \\ & \underline{\mathsf{s}} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p, \; [\vec{c}_1]_1 := [\vec{a}s]_1 \in \mathbb{G}_1^2, \; \mathsf{C} := \mathsf{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}, \vec{x}) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+1)\times|\mathsf{ct}|}, \; [\mathbf{C}_2]_1 := [(\mathbf{W}_0^\top \vec{c}_1|\ldots|\mathbf{W}_n^\top \vec{c}_1)\mathbf{C}]_1 \in \mathbb{G}_1^{3\times|\mathsf{ct}|}. \; \mathsf{Return} \\ & (\mathsf{part}(\mathsf{P}, \vec{x}), [\vec{c}_1]_1, [\mathbf{C}_2]_1). \\ & \underline{\mathsf{KeyGen}}(\mathsf{msk}, \mathsf{att}, \vec{y}) \colon \\ & \underline{\mathsf{r}} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p, \; [\vec{k}_1]_2 := [\vec{b}r]_2 \in \mathbb{G}_2^3, \; \mathbf{K} := \mathsf{EncKey}(\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+1)\times|\mathsf{sk}|}, \; [\mathbf{K}_2]_2 := [(\mathbf{W}_0 \vec{k}_1|\ldots|\mathbf{W}_n \vec{k}_1)\mathbf{K}]_2 \in \mathbb{G}_2^{2\times|\mathsf{sk}|}, \\ & [\vec{k}_3]_2 := [\mathbf{W}_0 \vec{k}_1]_2 \in \mathbb{G}_2^2. \; \mathsf{Return} \; (\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}, [\vec{k}_1]_2, [\mathbf{K}_2]_2, [\vec{k}_3]_2). \\ & \underline{\mathsf{Dec}}(\mathsf{part}(\mathsf{P}, \vec{x}), [\vec{c}_1]_1, [\mathbf{C}_2]_1, \vec{y}, [\vec{k}_1]_2, [\mathbf{K}_2]_2, [\vec{k}_3]_2) \colon \\ & [\vec{d}_1]_T := e([\mathbf{C}_2]_1^\top, [\vec{k}_1]_2) \in \mathbb{G}_2^{\mathsf{ct}}, \; [\vec{d}_2^\top]_T := e([\vec{c}_1]_1^\top, [\mathbf{K}_2]_2) \in \mathbb{G}_T^{1\times|\mathsf{sk}|}, \; \vec{d} := \mathsf{Decode}(\mathsf{part}(\mathsf{P}, \vec{x}), \mathsf{att}), [\gamma]_T := [(\vec{d}_1, \vec{d}_2)^\top \vec{d}]_T \in \mathbb{G}_T^\top \vec{k}_3 \cdot \mathsf{out}]_T. \; \mathsf{If} \; \mathsf{there} \; \mathsf{isn't} \; \mathsf{tsuch} \; \mathsf{out}, \; \mathsf{return} \perp. \end{split}
```

<span id="page-18-0"></span>**Fig. 7.** An adaptively-secure FE from pairings, for the function family  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d,B),\mathcal{P}}$ .

**Correctness.** Observe that for all predicates  $P \in \mathcal{P}$  and vectors  $\vec{x} \in [0, B]^d$ , the vector  $[(\mathbf{W}_0^\top \vec{c}_1 | \mathbf{W}_1^\top \vec{c}_1 | \dots | \mathbf{W}_n^\top \vec{c}_1)]_1 \in \mathbb{G}_1^{3 \times n}$  can be computed from mpk and the randomness  $s \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$  used by the encryption algorithm to compute  $[\vec{c}_1]_1 := [\vec{a}s]_1$ . Then, the encryption algorithm multiplies by the matrix  $\mathbf{C} := \operatorname{EncCt}(P, \vec{x}) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+1) \times |\operatorname{ct}|}$  to obtain  $[\mathbf{C}_2]_1 \in \mathbb{G}_1^{3 \times |\operatorname{ct}|}$ . Similarly, for all attributes att  $\in \mathcal{U}$ , the vector  $[(\mathbf{W}_0 \vec{k}_1 | \mathbf{W}_1 \vec{k}_1 | \dots | \mathbf{W}_n \vec{k}_1)]_2 \in \mathbb{G}_2^{2 \times n}$  can be computed from mpk, msk, and the randomness  $r \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$  used by the key generation algorithm to compute  $[\vec{k}_1]_2 := [\vec{b}r]_2$ . Then, the key generation algorithm multiplies by the matrix  $\mathbf{K} := \operatorname{EncKey}(\operatorname{att}, \vec{y}) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+1) \times |\operatorname{sk}|}$  to obtain  $[\mathbf{K}_2]_1 \in \mathbb{G}_2^{2 \times |\operatorname{sk}|}$ .

Let  $P \in \mathcal{P}$  and att  $\in \mathcal{U}$  such that  $P(\mathsf{att}) = 1$ ,  $\vec{x}, \vec{y} \in [0, B]^d$ ,  $(\mathsf{part}(\mathsf{P}, \vec{x}), [\vec{c}_1]_1, [\mathbf{C}_2]_1) \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{P}, \vec{x})$ , and  $(\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}, [\vec{k}_1]_2, [\vec{k}_2]_2, [\vec{k}_3]_2) \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{msk}, \mathsf{att}, \vec{y})$ . The values computed by the decryption algorithm are such

and 
$$(\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}, [k_1]_2, [\mathbf{K}_2]_2, [k_3]_2) \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{msk}, \mathsf{att}, \vec{y}).$$
 The values computed by the decryption algorithm are such that  $[\vec{d}_1]_T := \left[\mathbf{C}^{\top} \left( \frac{\vec{c}_1^{\top} \mathbf{W}_0 \vec{k}_1}{\vdots} \right) \right]_T$ , which implies that  $[\vec{d}_1^{\top}]_T = [(\vec{c}_1^{\top} \mathbf{W}_0 \vec{k}_1 | \vec{c}_1^{\top} \mathbf{W}_1 \vec{k}_1 | \dots | \vec{c}_1^{\top} \mathbf{W}_n \vec{k}_1) \mathbf{C}]_T \in \mathbb{G}_T^{1 \times |\mathsf{ct}|}$ ,

where  $\mathbf{C} := \mathsf{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}, \vec{x}) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+1) \times |\mathsf{ct}|}$ , and the second equality holds because  $\vec{c}_1^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{W}_i \vec{k}_1 \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ , for every  $i \in \{0 \dots n\}$ . Also,  $[\vec{d}_2^{\mathsf{T}}]_T := [(\vec{c}_1^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{W}_0 \vec{k}_1 | \vec{c}_1^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{W}_1 \vec{k}_1 | \dots | \vec{c}_1^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{W}_n \vec{k}_1) \mathbf{K}]_T \in \mathbb{G}_T^{1 \times |\mathsf{sk}|}$ , where  $\mathbf{K} := \mathsf{EncKey}(\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+1) \times |\mathsf{sk}|}$ .

{19}------------------------------------------------

Thus, by correctness of the function encoding (Param, EncCt, EncKey, Decode), we have  $[\gamma]_T := [\vec{c}_1^\top \mathbf{W}_0 \vec{k}_1 \cdot \vec{x}^\top \vec{y}]_T = [\vec{c}_1^\top \vec{k}_3 \cdot \vec{x}^\top \vec{y}] \in \mathbb{G}_T$ . Therefore, assuming the value  $B^2d$  is polynomial in the security parameter, the decryption can efficiently recover out  $= \vec{x}^\top \vec{y} \in [0, B^2d]$ .

```
 \begin{array}{c} \overline{\mathsf{G}_0, \boxed{\mathsf{G}_1 \boxed{\mathsf{G}_2 \boxed{:}}}} \\ \overline{\mathsf{G}_{\mathsf{G}}, \boxed{\mathsf{G}_1, \boxed{\mathsf{G}_2 \boxed{:}}}} \\ \overline{\mathsf{G}_{\mathsf{G}}, [0,1], \mathcal{P}\mathcal{G}} \leftarrow \mathsf{PGGen}(1^{\lambda}), \vec{a} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{DDH}, \vec{b} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3, (n, |\mathsf{ct}|, |\mathsf{sk}|) \leftarrow \mathsf{Param}(\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d,B)}, \mathcal{P}), \mathsf{for} \, \mathsf{all} \, i \in [0,n], \mathbf{W}_i \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{2\times 3}, \\ \mathsf{mpk} := ([\vec{a}]_1, \{[\mathbf{W}_i^\top \vec{a}]_1, \}_{i \in [n]}) \\ ((\mathsf{P}^0, \vec{x}^0), (\mathsf{P}^1, \vec{x}^1)) \leftarrow \mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{OKeyGen}(\cdot)}(1^{\lambda}, \mathsf{mpk}) \\ \mathsf{ct}^* \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{OEnc} \Big( (\mathsf{P}^0, \vec{x}^0), (\mathsf{P}^1, \vec{x}^1) \Big) \\ \beta' \leftarrow \mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{OKeyGen}(\cdot)}(\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{ct}^*) \\ \mathsf{Return} \, \mathsf{1} \, \mathsf{if} \, \beta' = \beta, \, \mathsf{0} \, \mathsf{otherwise}. \\ \\ \overline{\mathsf{OEnc}} \Big( (\mathsf{P}^0, \vec{x}^0), (\mathsf{P}^1, \vec{x}^1) \Big) \\ \vdots \\ \overline{\mathsf{S}} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p, \quad [\vec{c}_1]_1 \quad := \quad [\vec{a}s]_1, \quad [\vec{c}_1]_1 \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{G}_1^2 \quad \mathsf{C} \quad := \quad \mathsf{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^\beta, \vec{x}^\beta), \quad [\mathsf{C} := \mathsf{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^0, \vec{x}^0)], \quad [\mathsf{C}_2]_1 \quad := \\ [(\mathbf{W}_0^\top \vec{c}_1 | \mathbf{W}_1^\top \vec{c}_1 | \dots | \mathbf{W}_n^\top \vec{c}_1) \mathsf{C}]_T, \, \mathsf{Return} \, \mathsf{ct}^* := (\mathsf{part}(\mathsf{P}^\beta, \vec{x}^\beta), [\vec{c}_1]_1, [\mathsf{C}_2]_1) \\ \\ \overline{\mathsf{OKeyGen}}(\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}) \\ \vdots \\ \overline{\mathsf{C}} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p, \, [\vec{k}_1]_2 \quad := \quad [\vec{b}r]_1, \, \mathbf{K} \quad := \quad \mathsf{EncKey}(\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}), \, [\mathbf{K}_2]_2 \quad := \quad [(\mathbf{W}_0 \vec{k}_1 | \mathbf{W}_1 \vec{k}_1 | \dots | \mathbf{W}_k^\intercal)_1 \mathsf{K}]_2, \, [\vec{k}_3]_2 \quad := \quad [\mathbf{W}_0 \vec{k}_1]_2. \, \, \mathsf{Return} \\ (\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}, [\vec{k}_1]_2, [\mathbf{K}_2]_2, [\vec{k}_3]_2) \\ \end{array}
```

<span id="page-19-1"></span>**Fig. 8.** Hybrid games for the proof of Theorem 3.6.

<span id="page-19-0"></span>**Theorem 3.6 (AD-IND security).** If the underlying function encoding is secure, then the FE scheme from Fig. 7 is AD-IND secure. Namely, for any PPT adversary A, there exist PPT adversaries  $B_1$  and  $B_2$  such that:

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{AD\text{-}IND}}_{\mathcal{FE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{DDH}}_{\mathbb{G}_1,\mathcal{B}_1}(\lambda) + 4Q\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{DDH}}_{\mathbb{G}_2,\mathcal{B}_2}(\lambda),$$

where Q denotes the number of queries to OKeyGen.

*Proof.* The proof uses a series of hybrid games, described in Fig. 8. For each game G, we define by  $Adv_G(A)$  the advantage of A in G, that is:  $2 \cdot |\Pr[1 \leftarrow_R G(A)] - 1/2|$ .

**Game**  $G_0$ : is defined such that  $Adv_{G_0}(\mathcal{A}) = Adv_{\mathcal{FE},\mathcal{A}}^{AD\text{-IND}}(\lambda)$ .

**Game**  $G_1$ : here we change the distribution of the vector  $[\vec{c}_1]_1$  that is part of the challenge ciphertext to uniformly random over  $\mathbb{G}_1^2$ , using the DDH assumption in  $\mathbb{G}_1$ . Namely, we build a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_1$  such that:

$$|\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{G}_0}(\mathcal{A}) - \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{G}_1}(\mathcal{A})| \leq \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{DDH}}_{\mathbb{G}_1,\mathcal{B}_1}(\lambda).$$

Upon receiving a challenge  $(\mathcal{PG}, [\vec{a}]_1, [\vec{z}]_1)$ , where  $[\vec{z}]_1 := [\vec{a}s]_1$  for  $s \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$ , or  $[\vec{z}]_1 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{G}_1^2$ , the adversary  $\mathcal{B}_1$  samples  $(n, |\mathsf{ct}|, |\mathsf{sk}|) \leftarrow \mathsf{Param}(\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d,B)}, \mathcal{P})$ , for all  $i \in [0,n]$ ,  $\mathbf{W}_i \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{2 \times 3}$ , and simulate  $\mathcal{A}$ 's view in a straightforward way, setting  $[\vec{c}_1]_1 := [\vec{z}]_1$  in the challenge ciphertext.

**Game**  $G_2$ : here we change the distribution of the challenge ciphertext so that it doesn't depend on the random bit  $\beta \leftarrow_R \{0,1\}$  anymore. Clearly,

$$\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{G}_2}(\mathcal{A}) = 0.$$

We show that  $G_1$  and  $G_2$  are computationally indistinguishable using the security of a private-key variant of our scheme. Namely, we exhibit a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_2$  such that:

$$|\mathbf{Adv}_{\mathsf{G}_1}(\mathcal{A}) - \mathbf{Adv}_{\mathsf{G}_2}(\mathcal{A})| \leq \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_0}(\mathcal{B}_2),$$

{20}------------------------------------------------

where  $\operatorname{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_0}(\mathcal{B}_2)$  denotes the advantage of  $\mathcal{B}_2$  in game  $\mathsf{H}_0$ , which is the private-key analogue of game  $\mathsf{G}_0$  (see Fig. 10). We use the fact that for any  $i \in [0,n]$ :  $(\mathbf{W}_i^\top \vec{a}, \mathbf{W}_i^\top \vec{c}_1)$  with  $\mathbf{W}_i \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{2\times 3}$ ,  $\vec{a} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{DDH}$ ,  $\vec{c}_1 \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$ , is within negligible statistical distance from  $(\mathbf{W}_i^\top \vec{a}, \vec{w}_i)$  with  $\vec{w}_i \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$ . Roughly speaking, the vectors  $\vec{w}_i$  can be used as a fresh private-key, independent of the public key  $\{[\mathbf{W}_i^\top \vec{a}]_1\}$ . Note that when  $\vec{a} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{DDH}$  and  $\vec{a}^\perp \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2 \setminus \{\mathbf{0}\}$  such that  $\vec{a}^\top \vec{a}^\perp = 0$ , we have that the vectors  $(\vec{a}|\vec{a}^\perp)$  form a basis of  $\mathbb{Z}_p^2$ . Thus we can write  $\mathbf{W}_i^\top := \widehat{\vec{w}_i} \vec{a}^\top + \vec{w}_i (\vec{a}^\perp)^\top$ , where  $\widehat{\vec{w}_i}, \vec{w}_i \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$ , and  $\vec{a}^\perp \in \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  is such that  $\vec{a}^\top \vec{a}^\perp = 0$  and  $\vec{c}_1^\top \vec{a}^\perp = 1$ . This way, the public key can be written as:

$$\mathsf{mpk} := \left( [\vec{a}]_1, \{ [\widetilde{\vec{w}_i} \vec{a}^\top \vec{a}]_1 \}_{i \in [n]} \right),$$

the challenge ciphertext can be written as:

$$(\mathsf{part}(\mathsf{P}^\beta, \vec{x}^\beta), [\vec{c}_1]_1, [\mathbf{C}_2]_1), \text{ with } [\vec{c}_1]_1 \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{G}^2_1,$$
 
$$\mathbf{C} := \mathsf{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^\beta, \vec{x}^\beta),$$
 
$$[\mathbf{C}_2]_1 := [(\vec{w}_0^\top | \vec{w}_1^\top | \dots | \vec{w}_n^\top) \mathbf{C}]_1,$$

which corresponds exactly to game  $H_0$ . The functional decryption keys can be written as:

$$\begin{split} r &\leftarrow_{\mathbf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p, [\vec{k}_1]_2 := [\vec{b}r]_1, \mathbf{K} := \mathsf{EncKey}(\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}), \\ [\mathbf{K}_2]_2 :&= [(\vec{a}\widetilde{\vec{w}_0}^\top + \vec{a}^\perp \vec{w}_0^\top) \vec{k}_1 | \cdots | (\vec{a}\widetilde{\vec{w}_n}^\top + \vec{a}^\perp \vec{w}_n^\top) \vec{k}_1) \mathbf{K}]_2, \\ [\vec{k}_3]_2 :&= [(\vec{a}\widetilde{\vec{w}_0}^\top + \vec{a}^\perp \vec{w}_0^\top) \vec{k}_1]_2. \end{split}$$

The adversary  $\mathcal{B}_2$  samples  $\widetilde{w_i} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$  for all  $i \in [0,n]$  and  $\vec{a} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{DDH}$ ,  $\vec{a}^\perp \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^2$  such that  $\vec{a}^\top \vec{a}^\perp = 0$ , thanks to which it can simulate the public key to  $\mathcal{A}$ . To generate the challenge ciphertext,  $\mathcal{B}_2$  forwards the query  $\left((\mathsf{P}^0, \vec{x}^0), (\mathsf{P}^1, \vec{x}^1)\right)$  to its own encryption oracle, and forwards its challenge ciphertext to  $\mathcal{A}$ . When  $\mathcal{A}$  queries OKeyGen(att,  $\vec{y}$ ),  $\mathcal{B}_2$  queries its own oracle to get  $\mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} := (\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}, [\vec{k}_1]_2, [\vec{k}_2]_2, [k_3]_2)$ , where  $[\vec{k}_2^\top]_2 := [(\vec{w}_0^\top \vec{k}_1| \dots |\vec{w}_n^\top \vec{k}_1)\mathbf{K}]_2$  for  $\mathbf{K} := \mathsf{EncKey}(\mathsf{att}, \vec{y})$ , and  $[k_3]_2 := [\vec{w}_0^\top \vec{k}_1]_2$ .  $\mathcal{B}_2$  computes  $[\mathbf{K}_2']_2 := [\vec{a}^\perp \vec{k}_2^\top]_2 + [\vec{a}(\vec{w}_0^\top \mid \dots \mid \vec{w}_n^\top)\mathbf{K}]_2$ , and  $[\vec{k}_3']_2 := [\vec{a}^\perp k_3]_2 + [\vec{a}\vec{w}_0^\top \vec{k}_1]_2$ , and returns  $([\vec{k}_1]_2, [\mathbf{K}_2']_2, [\vec{k}_3']_2)$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ . In Lemma 3.7, we show that  $\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_0}(\mathcal{B}_2)$  is negligible.

<span id="page-20-0"></span>**Lemma 3.7.** For any PPT adversary A, there exists a PPT adversary B such that:

$$\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_0}(\mathcal{A}) \leq 4Q \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{DDH}}_{\mathbb{G}_2,\mathcal{B}}(\lambda),$$

where Q denotes the number of queries to OKeyGen.

*Proof.* The proof uses a series of hybrid games, described in Figs. 9 and 10. For any game H, we denote by  $Adv_H(A)$  the advantage of A in H, that is, the probability  $Pr[1 \leftarrow_R H(A)]$  that the game H returns 1 when interacting with A, where the probability is taken over the random coins of A and the game H itself.

**Game** H<sub>0</sub>: is described in Fig. 10. We use the basis  $(\vec{b}^*|\vec{b}_2^*|\vec{b}_3^*)$  of  $\mathbb{Z}_p^3$  to write  $\vec{w}_i \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$  as  $\vec{w}_i := w_i^1 \vec{b}^* + w_i^2 \vec{b}_1^* + w_i^3 \vec{b}_3^*$ , with  $w_i^1, w_i^2, w_i^3 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$ , for all  $i \in [0, n]$ . This basis is dual to the random basis  $(\vec{b}|\vec{b}_2|\vec{b}_3)$ , that is:  $\vec{b}_2^{\top} \vec{b}^* = \vec{b}_3^{\top} \vec{b}^* = 0$ ,  $\vec{b}^{\top} \vec{b}^* = 1$ ,  $\vec{b}^{\top} \vec{b}_2^* = \vec{b}_3^{\top} \vec{b}_2^* = 0$ ,  $\vec{b}_2^{\top} \vec{b}_2^* = 1$ ,  $\vec{b}^{\top} \vec{b}_3^* = \vec{b}_2^{\top} \vec{b}_3^* = 0$ ,  $\vec{b}_3^{\top} \vec{b}_3^* = 1$ .

**Game**  $H_1$ : we change the component of the ciphertext along the vector  $\vec{b}_3^*$ , using the one-time security of the function encoding. Namely, we use the fact that  $(w_0^3|\dots|w_n^3)\mathbf{C}^\beta$  is identically distributed to  $(w_0^3|\dots|w_n^3)\mathbf{C}^0$ , where  $w_0^3,\dots,w_n^3\leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}}\mathbb{Z}_p$ ,  $\mathbf{C}^\beta:=\operatorname{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^\beta,\vec{x}^\beta)$ , and  $\mathbf{C}^0:=\operatorname{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^0,\vec{x}^0)$ , by security of the function encoding (see Definition 3.5). Note that we rely on the fact that the values  $(w_0^3|\dots|w_n^3)$  do not appear in the functional decryption keys, since all of these keys contain vectors  $[\vec{k}_1]_2$  such that  $\vec{k}_1^\top \vec{b}_3^* = 0$ .

**Game**  $H_{1,q}$ : is defined in Fig. 9 for all  $q \in [Q]$  where Q denotes the number of queries to OKeyGen. Note that  $H_{1,0}$  is the same as game  $H_1$ . To show that  $H_{1,q-1}$  is computationally indistinguishable from  $H_{1,q}$  for all  $q \in [Q]$ , we use intermediate hybrid games  $H_{1,q-1,1}, H_{1,q-1,2}$ , and  $H_{1,q-1,3}$ , also defined in Fig. 9.

{21}------------------------------------------------

Game  $\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1,1}$ : in this game, we switch the distribution of the output to the q-th query to OKeyGen. Namely, the vector  $[\vec{k}_1]_2$  is sampled uniformly in  $\mathsf{span}([\vec{b}_2]_2)$  instead of  $\mathsf{span}([\vec{b}]_2)$ . This is done using the DDH assumption in  $\mathbb{G}_2$ . The reduction from DDH must not know the vectors  $\vec{b}^*$  and  $\vec{b}_2^*$ , which are trapdoors for the DDH assumption (they permit to test membership in  $\mathsf{span}([\vec{b}]_2)$  or  $\mathsf{span}([\vec{b}_2]_2)$  respectively). Indeed, it is possible to sample the vectors  $w_i^1 \vec{b}^* + w_i^2 \vec{b}_2^*$  with  $w_i^1, w_i^2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$  for all  $i \in [0, n]$ , without knowing explicitly the vectors  $\vec{b}^*$  and  $\vec{b}_2^*$ . We consider an intermediate game  $\mathsf{H}'_{1,q-1,1}$  which is like  $\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1,1}$  except that the vector  $[\vec{k}_1]_2$  from the q-th key is sampled uniformly over  $\mathsf{span}([\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_2]_2)$ . We build PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{B}'_{1,q-1}$  and  $\mathcal{B}'_{1,q-1,1}$  such that:  $|\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1,1}}(\mathcal{A}) - \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}'_{1,q-1,1}}(\mathcal{A})| \leq \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{As}-1}_{\mathcal{PG},\mathcal{B}'_{1,q-1}}(\lambda)$ , and  $|\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}'_{1,q-1,1}}(\mathcal{A}) - \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1,1}}(\mathcal{A})| \leq \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{As}-2}_{\mathcal{PG},\mathcal{B}'_{1,q-1,1}}(\lambda)$ . By Lemma 3.8, this implies the existence of a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_{1,q-1}$  such that

$$|\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1}}(\mathcal{A}) - \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1,1}}(\mathcal{A})| \leq 2 \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{DDH}}_{\mathbb{G}_2,\mathcal{B}_{1,q-1}}(\lambda).$$

Upon receiving  $(\vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2, \vec{b}_3^*, [\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2, [\vec{z}]_2)$ ,  $\mathcal{B}'_{1,q-1}$  computes the challenge ciphertext as  $\vec{c}_2^\beta + \vec{c}_2^0$  with  $\vec{c}_2^{\beta\top} := (\vec{u}_1^\top w_0^1 + \vec{u}_2^\top w_0^2 | \dots | \vec{u}_1^\top w_n^1 + \vec{u}_2^\top w_n^2) \mathbf{C}^\beta$  and  $\vec{c}_2^{0\top} := (\vec{b}_3^{*\top} w_0^3 | \dots | \vec{b}_3^{*\top} w_n^3) \mathbf{C}^0$  with  $w_i^1, w_i^2, w_i^3 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$  for all  $i \in [0, n]$ ,  $\mathbf{C}^\beta := \mathsf{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^\beta, \vec{x}^\beta)$  and  $\mathbf{C}^0 := \mathsf{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^0, \vec{x}^0)$ . It computes the keys as follows. For the q-1 first keys, it samples  $[\vec{k}_1]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{span}([\vec{b}_3]_2)$ ; for the q-th key, it uses  $[\vec{k}_1]_2 := [\vec{z}]_2$ , and for the last Q - q - 1 keys it computes  $[\vec{k}_1]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{span}([\vec{b}]_2)$ . The rest of the keys are computed as  $[\vec{k}_2^\top]_2 := [(\vec{w}_0^\top \vec{k}_1 | \vec{w}_1^\top \vec{k}_1 | \cdots | \vec{w}_n^\top \vec{k}_1) \mathbf{K}]_T$ ,  $[k_3]_2 := [\vec{w}_0^\top \vec{k}_1]_2$ , with  $\vec{w}_i := \vec{u}_1 w_i^1 + \vec{u}_2 w_i^2 + \vec{b}_3^* w_i^3$  for all  $i \in [0, n]$ . The adversary  $\mathcal{B}'_{1,q-1,1}$  works similarly. Game  $\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1,2}$ : Here we switch the distribution of the challenge ciphertext, using the fact that the following distributions are identical:

$$(w_0^2|\ldots|w_0^2)^{\top}\mathbf{C}^{\beta}, (w_0^2|\ldots|w_0^2)^{\top}\mathbf{K} \text{ and } (w_0^2|\ldots|w_0^2)^{\top}\mathbf{C}^0, (w_0^2|\ldots|w_0^2)^{\top}\mathbf{K},$$

where  $w_0^2, \ldots, w_n^2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$ ,  $\mathbf{C}^\beta := \operatorname{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^\beta, \vec{x}^\beta)$ ,  $\mathbf{C}^0 := \operatorname{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^0, \vec{x}^0)$ , and  $\mathbf{K} := \operatorname{EncKey}(\operatorname{att}, \vec{y})$ . This holds by security of the function encoding (see Definition 3.5), since by definition of the security game, we have  $\mathsf{P}^0(\operatorname{att}) \cdot \vec{y}^\top \vec{x}^0 = \mathsf{P}^1(\operatorname{att}) \cdot \vec{y}^\top \vec{x}^1$  for all queries  $(\operatorname{att}, \vec{y})$  to OKeyGen. The leftmost distribution corresponds to game  $\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1,1}$ , whereas the rightmost distribution corresponds to the game  $\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1,2}$ . Thus, we have:

$$Adv_{H_{1,q-1,1}}(A) = Adv_{H_{1,q-1,2}}(A).$$

**Game**  $H_{1,q-1,3}$ : in this game, we switch the distribution of the output to the q-th query to OKeyGen. Namely, the vector  $[\vec{k}_1]_2$  is sampled uniformly in  $\operatorname{span}([\vec{b}_3]_2)$  instead of  $\operatorname{span}([\vec{b}_2]_2)$ . This is done using the DDH assumption in  $\mathbb{G}_2$ . As for the transition between games  $H_{1,q-1,1}$  and  $H_{1,q-1,2}$ , we consider an intermediate game  $H'_{1,q-1,2}$  which is like  $H_{1,q-1,2}$  except the vector  $[\vec{k}_1]_2$  from the q-th key is sampled uniformly over  $\operatorname{span}([\vec{b}_2]_2,[\vec{b}_3]_2)$ . We build PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{B}'_{1,q-1,2}$  and  $\mathcal{B}'_{1,q-1,3}$  such that:  $|\operatorname{Adv}_{H_{1,q-1,2}}(\mathcal{A})-\operatorname{Adv}_{H'_{1,q-1,2}}(\mathcal{A})| \leq \operatorname{Adv}^{\operatorname{As}-4}_{\mathcal{PG},\mathcal{B}'_{1,q-1,2}}(\lambda)$ , and  $|\operatorname{Adv}_{H'_{1,q-1,2}}(\mathcal{A})-\operatorname{Adv}_{H_{1,q-1,3}}(\mathcal{A})| \leq \operatorname{Adv}^{\operatorname{As}-4}_{\mathcal{PG},\mathcal{B}'_{1,q-1,3}}(\lambda)$ . By Lemma 3.8, this implies the existence of a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_{1,q-1,2}$  such that

$$|\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1,2}}(\mathcal{A}) - \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1,3}}(\mathcal{A})| \leq 2 \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{DDH}}_{\mathbb{G}_2,\mathcal{B}_{1,q-1,2}}(\lambda).$$

Upon receiving  $(\vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2, \vec{b}^*, [\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2, [\vec{z}]_2)$ ,  $\mathcal{B}'_{1.q-1}$  computes the challenge ciphertext as  $\vec{c}_2^{\beta} + \vec{c}_2^0$  with  $\vec{c}_2^{\beta \top} := (\vec{b}^* \vec{b}^* w_0^1 | \dots | \vec{b}^* \vec{b}^* w_n^1) \mathbf{C}^{\beta}$  and  $\vec{c}_2^{0 \top} := (\vec{u}_1^\top w_0^2 + \vec{u}_2^\top w_0^3 | \dots | \vec{u}_1^\top w_n^2 + \vec{u}_2^\top w_n^3) \mathbf{C}^0$  with  $w_i^1, w_i^2, w_i^3 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$  for all  $i \in [0, n]$ . It computes the keys as follows. For the q-1 first keys, it samples  $[\vec{k}_1]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathrm{span}([\vec{b}_3]_2)$ ; for the q-th key, it uses  $[\vec{k}_1]_2 := [\vec{z}]_2$ , and for the last Q - q - 1 keys it computes  $[\vec{k}_1]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathrm{span}([\vec{b}]_2)$ . The rest of the keys are computed as  $[\vec{k}_2^\top]_2 := [(\vec{w}_0^\top \vec{k}_1 | \vec{w}_1^\top \vec{k}_1 | \cdots | \vec{w}_n^\top \vec{k}_1) \mathbf{K}]_T$ ,  $[k_3]_2 := [\vec{w}_0^\top \vec{k}_1]_2$ , with  $\vec{w}_i := \vec{b}^* w_i^1 + \vec{u}_1 w_i^2 + \vec{u}_2 w_i^3$  for all  $i \in [0, n]$ . The adversary  $\mathcal{B}'_{1,q-1,3}$  works similarly.

**Game**  $H_{1,q}$ : the transition between games  $H_{1,q-1,3}$  and  $H_{1,q}$  is similar to the transition between games  $H_0$  and  $H_1$ . Namely,we switch the distribution of the challenge ciphertext, using the fact that the following distributions are identical:

$$(w_0^2|\dots|w_0^2)^{\top} \mathbf{C}^{\beta} \text{ and } (w_0^2|\dots|w_0^2)^{\top} \mathbf{C}^0,$$

{22}------------------------------------------------

where  $w_0^2, \ldots, w_n^2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$ ,  $\mathbf{C}^{\beta} := \operatorname{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^{\beta}, \vec{x}^{\beta})$ , and  $\mathbf{C}^0 := \operatorname{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^0, \vec{x}^0)$ . The leftmost distribution corresponds to game  $\mathsf{H}_{1,q}$ , whereas the rightmost distribution corresponds to the game  $\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1,3}$ . Thus, we have:

$$\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_{1,q-1,3}}(\mathcal{A}) = \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_{1,q}}(\mathcal{A}).$$

**Game**  $H_2$ : the transition between games  $H_{1,Q}$  and  $H_2$  is also similar to the transition between games  $H_0$  and  $H_1$ . Namely, we switch the distribution of the challenge ciphertext, using the fact that the following distributions are identical:

$$(w_0^1, \dots, |w_0^1|)^{\top} \mathbf{C}^{\beta}$$
 and  $(w_0^1, \dots, |w_0^1|)^{\top} \mathbf{C}^0$ ,

where  $w_0^1, \ldots, w_n^1 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$ ,  $\mathbf{C}^{\beta} := \mathsf{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^{\beta}, \vec{x}^{\beta})$ , and  $\mathbf{C}^0 := \mathsf{EncCt}(\mathsf{P}^0, \vec{x}^0)$ . The leftmost distribution corresponds to game  $\mathsf{H}_{1.Q}$ , whereas the rightmost distribution corresponds to the game  $\mathsf{H}_2$ . Thus, we have:

$$\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_{1,O}}(\mathcal{A}) = \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{H}_2}(\mathcal{A}).$$


{23}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-23-1"></span>**Assumptions.** All assumptions are relative to a pairing group  $\mathcal{PG} = (\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2, \mathbb{G}_T, p, P_1, P_2, e) \leftarrow \mathsf{PGGen}(1^\lambda)$  and two random dual basis  $(\vec{b}|\vec{b}_2|\vec{b}_3)$  and  $(\vec{b}^*|\vec{b}_2^*|\vec{b}_3^*)$ , that is, the vectors are sampled randomly subject to:  $\vec{b}_2^\top \vec{b}^* = \vec{b}_3^\top \vec{b}^* = 0$ ,  $\vec{b}_3^\top \vec{b}^* = 1$ ,  $\vec{b}^\top \vec{b}_2^* = \vec{b}_3^\top \vec{b}_2^* = 0$ ,  $\vec{b}_2^\top \vec{b}_2^* = 1$ ,  $\vec{b}^\top \vec{b}_3^* = \vec{b}_2^\top \vec{b}_3^* = 0$ ,  $\vec{b}_3^\top \vec{b}_3^* = 1$ . For any assumption i and PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we define the advantage  $\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{PG},\mathcal{A}}^{\mathsf{As}-\mathsf{i}}(\lambda)$  accordingly.

**Assumption 1:** 
$$\left(\vec{u}_{1}, \vec{u}_{2} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}(\vec{b}^{*}, \vec{b}_{2}^{*}), \vec{b}_{3}^{*}, [\vec{b}]_{2}, [\vec{b}_{3}]_{2}, [\vec{z}]_{2} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}([\vec{b}]_{2})\right) \approx_{c} \left(\vec{u}_{1}, \vec{u}_{2} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}(\vec{b}^{*}, \vec{b}_{2}^{*}), \vec{b}_{3}^{*}, [\vec{b}]_{2}, [\vec{b}_{3}]_{2}, [\vec{z}]_{2} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}([\vec{b}]_{2}, [\vec{b}_{2}]_{2})\right).$$

**Assumption 2:** 
$$(\vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}(\vec{b}^*, \vec{b}_2^*), \vec{b}_3^*, [\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2, [\vec{z}]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}([\vec{b}_2]_2)) \approx_c (\vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}(\vec{b}^*, \vec{b}_2^*), \vec{b}_3^*, [\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2, [\vec{z}]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}([\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_2]_2)).$$

**Assumption 3:** 
$$(\vec{b}^*, \vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}(\vec{b}_2^*, \vec{b}_3^*), [\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2, [\vec{z}]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}([\vec{b}_2]_2)) \approx_c (\vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}(\vec{b}^*, \vec{b}_2^*), \vec{b}_3^*, [\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2, [\vec{z}]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}([\vec{b}_2]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2)).$$

$$\begin{array}{lll} \textbf{Assumption 4:} & \left( \vec{b}^*, \vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}(\vec{b}_2^*, \vec{b}_3^*), [\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2, \boxed{[\vec{z}]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}([\vec{b}_3]_2)} \right) \approx_c \\ & \left( \vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}(\vec{b}^*, \vec{b}_2^*), \vec{b}_3^*, [\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2, \boxed{[\vec{z}]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{span}([\vec{b}_2]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2)} \right). \end{aligned}$$

<span id="page-23-0"></span>**Lemma 3.8** (Reduction from DDH to the assumptions [OT09, Lew12]). For all the above assumptions, there is a reduction from DDH in  $\mathbb{G}_2$ .

*Proof.* Let  $\mathcal{A}$  be a PPT adversary. For each assumption i, we build a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_i$  that has a greater advantage in breaking DDH in  $\mathbb{G}_2$  that the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in breaking Assumption i. Upon receiving  $(\mathcal{PG}, [\vec{a}]_2, [\vec{t}]_2)$ , where  $\mathcal{PG} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{PGGen}(1^{\lambda}), \vec{a} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{DDH}, [\vec{t}]_2 := [\vec{a}s]_2$  with  $s \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$ , or  $[\vec{t}]_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{G}_2^2$ , adversary  $\mathcal{B}_i$  does the following:

Assumption 1:  $\mathcal{B}_1$  samples  $\vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2, \vec{b}_3^* \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$ , compute  $[\widetilde{a}]_2$  such that  $\begin{pmatrix} \vec{a} \\ \vec{a} \end{pmatrix}^\top \vec{b}_3^* = 0$ . Writing  $\vec{b}_3^* := \begin{pmatrix} \alpha \\ \beta \\ \gamma \end{pmatrix}$  and assuming  $\gamma \neq 0$ , this can be done efficiently by setting  $[\widetilde{a}]_2 := \frac{-[\vec{a}]_2^\top \binom{\alpha}{\beta}}{\gamma}$ . Similarly, compute  $[\widetilde{t}]_2 := \frac{-[\vec{t}]_2^\top \binom{\alpha}{\beta}}{\gamma}$ . Set  $[\vec{b}]_2 := \begin{bmatrix} \vec{a} \\ \widetilde{a} \end{bmatrix}_2$ ,  $[\vec{z}]_2 := \begin{bmatrix} \vec{z} \\ \widetilde{z} \end{bmatrix}_2$  sample  $\vec{b}_3 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$  such that  $\vec{b}_3^\top \vec{b}_3^* = 0$  and return  $(\vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2, \vec{b}_3^*, [\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2, [\vec{z}]_2)$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ .

**Assumption 2:**  $\mathcal{B}_2$  samples  $\vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2, \vec{b}_3^* \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$ , compute  $[\tilde{\vec{z}}]_2$  as  $\mathcal{B}_1$ . Set  $[\vec{z}]_2 := \begin{bmatrix} \vec{z} \\ \widetilde{z} \end{bmatrix}_2$ , sample  $\vec{b}, \vec{b}_3 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$  such that  $\vec{b}^{\top} \vec{b}_3^* = \vec{b}_3^{\top} \vec{b}_3^* = 0$ , and return  $(\vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2, \vec{b}_3^*, [\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2, [\vec{z}]_2)$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ .

**Assumption 3:**  $\mathcal{B}_3$  samples  $\vec{b}^*, \vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$ , compute  $[\widetilde{z}]_2$  such that  $\begin{pmatrix} \vec{z} \\ \widetilde{z} \end{pmatrix}^\top \vec{b}^* = 0$ , which can be done efficiently, as  $\mathcal{B}_1$  (replacing  $\vec{b}_3^*$  with  $\vec{b}^*$ ). Set  $[\vec{z}]_2 := \begin{bmatrix} \vec{z} \\ \widetilde{z} \end{bmatrix}_2$ , sample  $\vec{b}, \vec{b}_3 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$  such that  $\vec{b}_3^\top \vec{b}_3^* = 0$  and  $\vec{b}^\top \vec{b}^* = 1$ , and return  $(\vec{b}^*, \vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2, [\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2, [\vec{z}]_2)$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ .

**Assumption 4:**  $\mathcal{B}_4$  samples  $\vec{b}^*, \vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$ , compute  $[\widetilde{a}]_2$  and  $[\widetilde{z}]_2$  such that  $\begin{pmatrix} \vec{a} \\ \vec{a} \end{pmatrix}^\top \vec{b}^* = 0$  and  $\begin{pmatrix} \vec{z} \\ \vec{z} \end{pmatrix}^\top \vec{b}^* = 0$ , respectively. Set  $[\vec{b}_3]_2 := \begin{bmatrix} \vec{a} \\ \vec{a} \end{bmatrix}_2$ ,  $[\vec{z}]_2 := \begin{bmatrix} \vec{z} \\ \vec{z} \end{bmatrix}_2$ , sample  $\vec{b} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^3$  such that  $\vec{b}^\top \vec{b}^* = 1$ , and return  $(\vec{b}^*, \vec{u}_1, \vec{u}_2, [\vec{b}]_2, [\vec{b}_3]_2, [\vec{z}]_2)$  to  $\mathcal{A}$ .

{24}------------------------------------------------


<span id="page-24-2"></span><span id="page-24-1"></span>Fig. 10. Hybrid games for the proofs of Theorem 3.6 and Lemma 3.7.

## <span id="page-24-0"></span>4 A Lattice-Based Identity-Based Functional Encryption in the Random-Oracle Model

In this section, we build an identity-based functional encryption (IFE) for the inner-product functionality from LWE in the random-oracle model. In Section 5, we provide a lattice-based scheme that is proven secure in the standard model.

#### 4.1 Lattice Preliminaries

**Matrix norms** For a vector  $\vec{u}$ ,  $||\vec{u}||$  denotes its  $\ell_2$  norm. For a matrix  $\mathbf{R}$ , by  $\mathbf{R}$  we denote the result of applying Gram Schmidt orthogonalization on the columns of  $\mathbf{R}$ . In addition:

- $\|\mathbf{R}\|$  denotes the  $\ell_2$  norm of the longest column of  $\mathbf{R}$ .
- $\|\mathbf{R}\|_2$  denotes the operator norm of  $\mathbf{R}$ , where  $\|\mathbf{R}\|_2 = \sup_{\|\vec{x}\|=1} \|\mathbf{R}\vec{x}\|$ , with  $\vec{x} \in \mathbb{Z}^m$
- $s_1(\mathbf{R})$  denotes the spectral norm (the largest singular value of  $\mathbf{R}$ ).

Known facts are that  $\|\widetilde{\mathbf{R}}\| \le \|\mathbf{R}\| \le \|\mathbf{R}\|_2 \le \sqrt{k}\|\mathbf{R}\|$ . We will also need the fact that concatenating two matrices  $\mathbf{R}$ ,  $\mathbf{S}$  yields  $s_1(\mathbf{R}|\mathbf{S}) \le \sqrt{s_1(\mathbf{R})^2 + s_1(\mathbf{S}^2)}$ . For bounding the spectral norm, we will require the following lemma:

**Lemma 4.1.** [DM14] Let  $\mathbf{X} \in \mathbb{R}^{n \times m}$  be a subgaussian random matrix with parameter s. There exists a universal constant  $C \approx \frac{1}{\sqrt{2\pi}}$  such that for any  $t \geq 0$ , we have  $s_1(\mathbf{X}) \leq C \cdot s \cdot (\sqrt{m} + \sqrt{n} + t)$  except with probability at most  $\frac{2}{e^{\pi t^2}}$ .

**Lattices.** For any matrix  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times m}$  and any vector  $\vec{p} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^n$ , we define the orthogonal q-ary lattice of  $\mathbf{A}$ :  $\Lambda_q^{\perp}(\mathbf{A}) := \{\vec{u} \in \mathbb{Z}^m : \mathbf{A}\vec{u} = \vec{0} \bmod q\}$  and the shifted lattice:  $\Lambda_q^{\vec{p}}(\mathbf{A}) := \{\vec{u} \in \mathbb{Z}^m : \mathbf{A}\vec{u} = \vec{p} \bmod q\}$ . Similarly, for any matrix  $\mathbf{P} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times \ell}$ , we define:  $\Lambda_q^{\mathbf{P}}(\mathbf{A}) := \{\mathbf{U} \in \mathbb{Z}^{m \times \ell} : \mathbf{A}\mathbf{U} = \mathbf{P} \bmod q\}$ .

{25}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-25-2"></span>**Probability distributions** Consider a lattice  $\Lambda \subseteq \mathbb{Z}^m$ . The normal Gaussian distribution of mean 0 and variance  $\sigma^2$  is the distribution on  $\mathbb{R}$  with probability density function  $\frac{1}{\sigma \cdot \sqrt{2\pi}} \frac{1}{e^{x^2/(2\sigma^2)}}$ . The lattice gaussian distribution with support  $\Lambda \subseteq \mathbb{R}^n$ , standard deviation  $\sigma$  and centered at  $\vec{c}$  is defined as:

for all 
$$\vec{y} \in \Lambda : D_{\Lambda,\sigma,\vec{c}}(\vec{y}) = \frac{e^{-\pi \|\vec{y} - \vec{c}\|^2/\sigma^2}}{\sum_{\vec{x} \in \Lambda} e^{-\pi \|\vec{x} - \vec{c}\|^2/\sigma^2}}$$

<span id="page-25-1"></span>**Lemma 4.2** (Bounding Gaussian Noise). [MR04] For any n-dimensional lattice  $\Lambda$ ,  $\vec{c} \in span(\Lambda)$ , real  $\epsilon \in (0,1)$ , and  $s \geq \eta_{\epsilon}(\Lambda)$ ,

$$\Pr_{x \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathcal{D}_{A,s,c}} [\|\vec{x} - \vec{c}\| > s\sqrt{n}] \le \frac{1+\epsilon}{1-\epsilon} \frac{1}{2^n}.$$

Also from [MR04, Lemma 3.2], for any n-dimensional lattice  $\Lambda$ ,  $\eta_{\epsilon}(\Lambda) \leq \sqrt{n}/\lambda_1(\Lambda^*)$ , where  $\epsilon = \frac{1}{2^n}$ . This was improved by [GPV08] to obtain that for any  $\omega(\sqrt{\log n})$  function, there is a negligible  $\epsilon(n)$  such that:  $\eta_{\epsilon}(\mathbb{Z}) \leq \omega(\sqrt{\log n})$ . In particular, when sampling integers, we have that for any  $\epsilon \in (0, \frac{1}{2})$  any  $s \geq \eta_{\epsilon}(\mathbb{Z})$ , and any  $t \geq \omega(\sqrt{\log n})$ :

$$\Pr_{x \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}, s, c}}[|x - c| > s \cdot t] \le \mathsf{negl}(n).$$

**Learning with errors** (LWE) [Reg05] Let q be a prime,  $\chi$  be a public distribution over  $\mathbb{Z}_q$  and  $\vec{s}$  be uniformly random over  $\mathbb{Z}_q^n$ . Moreover,  $\vec{s}$  is constant across calls to oracles  $\mathcal{O}_{\vec{l}}$  or  $\mathcal{O}_{\$}$ , defined below:

- Oracle  $\mathcal{O}_{\vec{s}}$  outputs samples  $(\vec{a}, \vec{a}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{s} + \vec{x})$ , where  $\vec{a} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_q^n$  and  $x \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \chi$  are fresh and independently sampled.
- Oracle  $\mathcal{O}_{\$}$  outputs uniformly random elements of  $\mathbb{Z}_q^n \times \mathbb{Z}_q$ .

Define another oracle  $\mathcal{O}$ , which across all calls, is either  $\mathcal{O}_{\vec{s}}$  or  $\mathcal{O}_{\$}$ . The learning with errors LWE $_{q,\chi,n}$  problem is to distinguish with non-negligible probability, given access to oracle  $\mathcal{O}$ , whether it corresponds to  $\mathcal{O}_{\vec{s}}$  or  $\mathcal{O}_{\$}$ .

**Proposition 4.3** ([Reg05]). Let  $\alpha = \alpha(n) \in (0,1)$  and let q = q(n) be a prime such that  $\alpha \cdot q > 2\sqrt{n}$ . If there exists an efficient (possibly quantum) algorithm that solves  $\mathsf{LWE}_{q,\bar{\psi}_{\alpha}}$ , then there exists an efficient quantum algorithm for approximating SIVP and GapSVP in the  $\ell_2$  norm, in the worst case, to within  $\widetilde{O}(n/\alpha)$  factors.

In [Pei07], this result has been generalized for any  $\ell_p$  norm, with  $2 \le p \le \infty$  and for similar  $\widetilde{O}(n/\alpha)$  approximation factors.

**Theorem 4.4.** [GPV08, Theorem 4.1] There is a probabilistic polynomial-time algorithm SampleD that, given a basis  $\mathbf{B}$  of an n-dimensional lattice  $\Delta = \mathcal{L}(\mathbf{B})$ , a parameter  $s \geq \|\widetilde{\mathbf{B}}\| \cdot \omega(\sqrt{\log n})$ , and a center  $\vec{c} \in \mathcal{R}$ , outputs a sample from a distribution that is statistically close to  $D_{\Lambda,s,\vec{c}}$ .

**Proposition 4.5.** [Ajt99] For any prime q = poly(n) and any  $m \ge 5n \lg q$ , there is a probabilistic polynomial-time algorithm SampleMat that, on input  $1^n$ , outputs a matrix  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times m}$  and a full-rank set  $\mathbf{S} \subset \Lambda_q^{\perp}(\mathbf{A})$ , where the distribution of  $\mathbf{A}$  is statistically close to uniform over  $\mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times m}$  and the length  $\|\mathbf{S}\| \le L = m^{2.5}$ .

Also, by [MG02, Lemma 7.1, page 129],  $\mathbf{S}$  can be converted efficiently to a "good" basis  $\mathbf{T}$  of  $\Lambda_q^{\perp}(\mathbf{A})$  such that  $\|\widetilde{\mathbf{T}}\| \leq \|\widetilde{\mathbf{S}}\| \leq L$ .

<span id="page-25-0"></span>**Lemma 4.6** ([GPV08]**Preimage Samplable Functions).** For any prime q = poly(n), any  $m \ge 5n \lg q$ , and any  $s \ge L \cdot \omega(\sqrt{\log m})$ , it holds that there exist PPT algorithms TrapGen, SampleD, SamplePre such that:

- 1. TrapGen computes  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{T}) \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{TrapGen}(1^n, 1^m)$ , where  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{m \times n}$  is statistically close to uniform and  $T \subset \Lambda_q^{\perp}(\mathbf{A})$  is a good basis with  $\|\widetilde{\mathbf{T}}\| \leq L$ . The matrix  $\mathbf{A}$  (and q) are public, while the good basis  $\mathbf{T}$  is the trapdoor.
- 2. SampleD samples vectors  $\vec{e}$  from  $\mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^{\ell \times m},s}$ .
- 3. The trapdoor inversion algorithm  $\mathsf{SamplePre}(\mathbf{A},\mathbf{T},s,\mathbf{U})$  outputs a matrix  $\mathbf{Z} \in \mathbb{Z}^{\ell \times m}$  such that  $\mathbf{U} = \mathbf{Z}\mathbf{A}$ .

In addition, it holds that the following distributions  $D_1, D_2$  are statistically close:

$$D_1 := (\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{Z}, \mathbf{U}), \text{ s.t. } (\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{T}) \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{TrapGen}(1^n, 1^m), \mathbf{U} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}^{\ell \times n}, \\ \mathbf{Z} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{SamplePre}(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{T}, s, \mathbf{U}) \\ D_2 := (\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{Z}, \mathbf{Z}\mathbf{A}), \text{ where } \mathbf{A} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_q^{m \times n}, \mathbf{Z} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^{\ell \times m}, s} : \|\vec{z}_i\| \leq s\sqrt{m}, i \in \{1 \dots m\} \\ \text{ where } \vec{z}_i \text{ denotes the } i\text{-th row of } \mathbf{Z}.$$

{26}------------------------------------------------

```
Setup(1^{\lambda}, \mathcal{X}, \mathcal{Y}):
                                                                                                                                              KeyGen(id, \vec{y}):
\overline{(\mathbf{A},\mathbf{T})} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \overline{\mathsf{Trap}} \mathsf{Gen}(1^n,1^m)
                                                                                                                                              \mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}} \leftarrow H(\mathsf{id})
                                                                                                                                              \mathbf{Z}_{\mathsf{id}} \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathsf{SamplePre}(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{T}, \rho, \mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}})
 \mathsf{mpk} \leftarrow \mathbf{A}, \mathsf{msk} \leftarrow \mathbf{T}
                                                                                                                                              Return (\vec{y}, \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{id}, \vec{y}} := (\vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \cdot \mathbf{Z}_{\mathsf{id}}))
 Enc(mpk, id, \vec{x}):
\mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}} \leftarrow \overline{H(\mathsf{id})}
                                                                                                                                              \mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{ct}_1,\mathsf{ct}_2,\mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{id},\vec{y}},\vec{y}):
                                                                                                                                              \overline{\mu = \vec{y}^{\top} \cdot \mathsf{ct}_2 - \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{id},\vec{y}} \cdot \mathsf{ct}_1}
\vec{s} \leftarrow_{\mathbf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_q^n
                                                                                                                                              \mu' = \arg\min_{\mu' \in \{0...K+1\}} \left| \left| \frac{q}{K} \right| \cdot \mu - \mu' \right|
\vec{f}_1 \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^m,\sigma}
\vec{f}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^\ell,\sigma}
                                                                                                                                              Return \mu'
\mathsf{ct}_1 \leftarrow \mathbf{A}\vec{s} + \vec{f}_1
\mathsf{ct}_2 = \mathbf{U}_\mathsf{id} \vec{s} + \vec{f}_2 + \left| \frac{q}{K} \right| \cdot \vec{x}
Return (ct_1, ct_2)
```

<span id="page-26-0"></span>**Fig. 11.** An identity-based inner-product functional encryption scheme  $\mathcal{IFE}$  in the random-oracle model, where H denotes the random oracle. Algorithms TrapGen and SamplePre are referenced in Lemma 4.6

**Noise Rerandomization.** The following procedure NoiseGen( $\mathbf{R}, s$ ) for noise rerandomization, was described in [KY16]. NoiseGen( $\mathbf{R}, s$ ): given a matrix  $\mathbf{R} \in \mathbb{Z}^{m \times t}$ , and  $s \in \mathbb{R}^+$  such that  $s^2 > s_1(\mathbf{R}\mathbf{R}^\top)$ , it first samples  $\vec{e}_1 := \mathbf{R}\vec{e} + (s^2\mathbf{I}_m - \mathbf{R}\mathbf{R}^\top)^{\frac{1}{2}}\vec{e}'$ , where  $\mathbf{I}_m \in \mathbb{Z}^{m \times m}$  denotes the identity matrix, and  $\vec{e} \leftarrow_{\mathbf{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\sigma}^t$  and  $\vec{e}' \leftarrow_{\mathbf{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\sqrt{2}\sigma}^m$  are independent spherical continuous Gaussian noises. Then, it samples  $\vec{e}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathbf{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^m - \vec{e}_1, s\sqrt{2}\sigma}$ , and returns  $\vec{e}_1 + \vec{e}_2 \in \mathbb{Z}_q^m$ . We have the following lemma:

<span id="page-26-2"></span>**Lemma 4.7** (Noise Distribution [KY16]). Let  $\mathbf{R} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}^{m \times t}$  and  $s > s_1(\mathbf{R})$ . The following distributions are statistically close:

**Distribution 1**:  $\vec{e} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^t,\sigma}$  and  $\vec{e}' \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{NoiseGen}(\mathbf{R},s)$ . Output  $\mathbf{R}\vec{e} + \vec{e}'$ .

**Distribution 2**: Output  $e \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^m,2s\sigma}$ .

#### **4.2** Our Construction

In this section, we describe how to obtain an identity-based inner-product funtional encryption scheme based on the hardness of LWE in the random-oracle model. Our idea is to start with a modification of the ALS functional encryption scheme for inner-products [ALS16], proposed by [WFL19] and which we recall in Appendix A. We modify the identity-based encryption scheme of [GPV08] in such a way as to support functional key generation queries, as in ALS. Our construction is described in Fig. 11. Ciphertexts encode vectors  $\vec{x} \in \mathcal{X} := \{0, \dots, P-1\}^{\ell}$  under an identity id. Secret keys correspond to an identity id and a vector  $\vec{y} \in \mathcal{Y} := \{0, \dots, V-1\}^{\ell}$ . When the identities match, our scheme decrypts the bounded inner-product  $\langle \vec{x}, \vec{y} \rangle \in \{0, \dots, K-1\}$  where  $K = \ell PV$ .

Since our construction achieves anonymity and the size of input vectors  $\vec{x}$  are fixed, no partial information about the input is leaked. That is,  $part(\vec{x}, id) = \bot$ .

**Lemma 4.8** (Correctness). For  $q \ge 2K\ell\sqrt{\ell}V\omega(\log^2 n)$ ,  $\sigma = 2C\alpha q(\sqrt{m} + \sqrt{n} + \sqrt{\ell})$ ,  $\rho \ge \omega(\sqrt{\log n})$ ,  $m = 2n\log q$ , the scheme from Fig. 11 is correct.

*Proof.* When identities match, observe that decryption yields  $\vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{U}\vec{s} + \vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\vec{f}_2 + \vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{Z}\mathbf{A}\vec{s} + \vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{Z}\vec{f}_1 + \left\lfloor \frac{q}{K} \right\rfloor \langle \vec{x}, \vec{y} \rangle$ , which is equal to:

$$\underbrace{\vec{y}^{\!\scriptscriptstyle \top} \vec{f_2} + \vec{y}^{\!\scriptscriptstyle \top} \mathbf{Z} \vec{f_1}}_{\text{error terms}} + \left\lfloor \frac{q}{K} \right\rfloor \langle \vec{x}, \vec{y} \rangle$$

This decrypts correctly as long as the error terms are small. From Lemma 4.2, we know that every entry of  $\mathbf{Z}$  is with overwhelming probability bounded by  $\omega(\log n)$ , so  $\|\mathbf{Z}\| \leq \sqrt{\ell} \cdot \omega(\log n)$ , as long as  $\rho \geq \omega(\sqrt{\log n})$ . We use Lemma 4.2 once again and we bound  $\|\vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}}\mathbf{Z}\vec{e}_1\| \leq \ell\sqrt{\ell}V\omega(\log^2 n)$  and  $\|\vec{y}\vec{e}_2\| \leq \ell V\omega(\sqrt{\log n})$ , as long as

{27}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-27-0"></span> $\sigma \geq \omega(\sqrt{\log n})$ . For decryption to succeed, we want that the error terms are smaller than  $\frac{q}{2K}$ , which implies:  $q \geq 2K\ell\sqrt{\ell}V\omega(\log^2 n)$ , which is the case for our choice of parameters.

Remark 4.9 (No smudging noise). We remark that in our setup, we rely on efficient lattice parameters and require no smudging or superpolynomial modulus.

**Theorem 4.10** (Security). Let n be the security parameter,  $q \geq 2K\ell\sqrt{\ell}V\omega(\log^2 n)$ ,  $\sigma = 2C\alpha q(\sqrt{m} + \sqrt{n} + \sqrt{\ell})$ ,  $\rho \geq \omega(\sqrt{\log n})$ ,  $m = 2n\log q$ ,  $\alpha \leq \frac{\sigma}{2C\alpha q(\sqrt{m} + \sqrt{n} + \sqrt{\ell})}$ , then the scheme from Fig. 11 is AD-IND-secure in the random-oracle model, assuming that  $\mathsf{LWE}_{q,\alpha,n}$  is hard.

*Proof.* We prove adaptive security in the random-oracle model, following the proof structure and techniques of [GPV08], while making several changes to adapt the proof techniques to functional encryption. First, without loss of generality, we assume that any adversary making key generation queries of the form  $id || \vec{y} |$  will first query the random oracle on id (we can make this assumption because for every adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we can compile it into an adversary  $\mathcal{A}'$  that exhibits this behavior). We proceed in a series of hybrids, consider  $\mathcal{A}$  to be a PPT adversary, and  $n \in \mathbb{N}$  to be the security parameter. We denote by  $\mathbf{Adv}_{\mathsf{Game}_i}(\mathcal{A})$  the advantage of  $\mathcal{A}$  in game i.

**Game 0** This is the original AD-IND game.

**Game 1** In this game, we guess what identities  $id_0^*$  and  $id_1^*$  will be used for the challenge messages. Guessing  $id_0^*$ ,  $id_1^*$  themselves would incur an exponential security loss. Therefore, instead of guessing the identities themselves, we guess the index of the random-oracle query in which the adversary queries the oracle H to get  $U_{id_0^*}$  and  $U_{id_1^*}$ . This will result in an  $\frac{1}{Q^2}$  security loss, where Q is the total number of queries to the random oracle. As explained above, we are guaranteed that the adversary will make this query.

Depending on whether  $id_0^* = id_1^*$ , we will either perform a reduction to the ALS scheme (see Appendix A) or directly to LWE.

Case 1,  $id_0^* = id_1^*$  We perform a reduction to the security of the ALS [ALS16] encryption scheme, which we recall in Appendix A. We reduce to the AD-IND security of ALS. We first obtain from the challenger public keys  $A_{ALS}$ ,  $D_{ALS}$ . Now, equipped with the knowledge of  $id^*$ , we define  $Game_1$  to be the same as  $Game_0$ , except for the following changes:

- Matrix A is not generated with TrapGen anymore, instead A is now replaced with  $A_{ALS}$ .
- For every id  $\neq$  id\* query to the random oracle H, we draw a matrix  $\mathbf{Z}_{id} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^{\ell \times m}, \rho}$ . We program the random oracle  $H(id) = \mathbf{U}_{id}$ , where  $\mathbf{U}_{id}$  is now computed as  $\mathbf{U}_{id} = \mathbf{Z}_{id} \mathbf{A}$ . Key queries of the form  $id \parallel \vec{y}$  are answered by returning  $\vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{Z}_{id}$ .
- For id = id\*, we program  $H(id) = \mathbf{D}_{ALS}$ .
- For key queries of the form  $\mathrm{id}^* \| \vec{y}$ , we notice that we cannot answer them ourselves. However, we are allowed to forward  $\vec{y}$  to the challenger of the AD-IND security of ALS, which replies with  $\vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbb{Z}_{\mathrm{ALS}}$ , where  $\mathbf{Z}_{\mathrm{ALS}}$  is the master secret key of the ALS scheme. We set  $\mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{id}^* \| \vec{y}} := \vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbb{Z}_{\mathrm{ALS}}$  and forward it to the adversary.
- When the adversary finally submits its challenge  $(\vec{x}_0, \vec{x}_1)$ , we forward this to the ALS challenger, which replies with ct :=  $(ct_1, ct_2)$ . We forward ct back to the adversary.

We therefore have that  $\mathbf{Adv}_{\mathsf{Game}_1}(\mathcal{A}) \leq \mathbf{Adv}_{\mathsf{ALS}}(\mathcal{A})$ , now we only need to show that  $|\mathbf{Adv}_{\mathsf{Game}_0}(\mathcal{A}) - \mathbf{Adv}_{\mathsf{Game}_1}(\mathcal{A})| \leq \mathsf{negl}(n)$ . For this we use Lemma 4.6, which tells us that:

For any id  $\neq$  id\*,  $D_0 := (\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{Z}_{\mathsf{id}}, \mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}})$ , where  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{T}) \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{TrapGen}(1^n)$ ,  $\mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}^{\ell \times n}$ ,  $\mathbf{Z}_{\mathsf{id}} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{SamplePre}(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{T}, s, \mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}})$ , (which is the same as the adversarial view in  $\mathsf{Game}_0$ ) is statistically close to  $D_1 := (\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{Z}_{\mathsf{id}}, \mathbf{Z}_{\mathsf{id}}, \mathbf{A})$ , where  $\mathbf{A} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_q^{m \times n}$ ,  $\mathbf{Z}_{\mathsf{id}} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^{\ell \times m}} : \|\vec{z}_i\| \leq s\sqrt{m}$  (recall that  $\vec{z}_i$  denotes the i-th row of  $\mathbf{Z}$ ). Note that in ALS, matrix  $\mathbf{A}_{\mathsf{ALS}}$  is indeed uniformly random, which corresponds to how  $\mathbf{A}$  is chosen in  $\mathsf{Game}_1$ .

{28}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-28-2"></span>Finally, when  $id = id^*$ , we need to argue that ALS public keys are distributed statistically close to honestly generated keys in  $Game_0$ . Note that in ALS, the second matrix  $\mathbf{D}_{ALS}$  is not uniformly random like in  $Game_0$ , but instead is of the form  $\mathbf{D}_{ALS} = \mathbf{Z}_{ALS} \mathbf{A}_{ALS}$  (in fact in can be proven that  $\mathbf{U}$  is not statistically close to uniformly random). However, this is not an issue in our case, as the distribution of  $\mathbf{U}_{id^*}$  is in fact uniform conditioned on it being of the form  $\mathbf{Z}_{id^*} \mathbf{A}$ . Therefore, we can apply Lemma 4.6 once again, and conclude that  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{Z}_{id^*}, \mathbf{U}_{id^*})$  with trapdoored  $\mathbf{A}$  and uniform  $\mathbf{U}_{id^*}$  is statistically close to  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{Z}_{id^*}, \mathbf{Z}_{id^*} \mathbf{A})$ .

Case 2,  $id_0^* \neq id_1^*$  In this case, we make the following observation: if the adversary was allowed to obtain secret keys for either  $id_0^* || \vec{y}$  or  $id_1^* || \vec{y}$ , for any  $\vec{y}$ , then it could trivially distinguish between encryptions of  $\vec{x}_0$  under  $id_0^*$  and encryptions of  $\vec{x}_1$  under  $id_1^*$ . This type of trivial attack is excluded by the AD-IND definition, therefore we conclude that the adversary cannot obtain decryption key queries for neither  $id_0^*$  or  $id_1^*$ . With this limitation, we can perform

a reduction directly to LWE for matrix  $\mathbf{B} = \left(\frac{\mathbf{A}}{\mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}_0^*}}\right) \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{(m+2\ell) \times n}$ , where  $\mathbf{B}$  is uniformly random. We obtain a

ciphertext  $\vec{c} = (\vec{c}_1, \vec{c}_2, \vec{c}_3)$  from the LWE oracle. To simulate the view of  $\mathcal{A}$ , we set  $H(\mathsf{id}_0^*) = \mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}_0^*}$  and  $H(\mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}_1^*})$ . For all the other keys, we proceed as in the previous case:

- 1. For every  $\mathsf{id} \notin \{\mathsf{id}_0^*, \mathsf{id}_1^*\}$  query to the random oracle H, we draw a matrix  $\mathbf{Z}_{\mathsf{id}} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^{\ell \times m}, \rho}$ . We program the random oracle  $H(\mathsf{id}) = \mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}}$ , where  $\mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}}$  is now computed as  $\mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}} = \mathbf{Z}_{\mathsf{id}} \mathbf{A}$ .
- 2. For every id  $\notin \{id_0^*, id_1^*\}$ , key queries of the form id  $||\vec{y}|$  are answered by returning  $\vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{Z}_{\mathsf{id}}$ .

Challenge queries to answer a challenge encryption for  $\vec{x}_0$  under  $\mathrm{id}_0^*$  or  $\vec{x}_1$  under  $\mathrm{id}_1^*$ , we pick uniformly at random a bit  $\beta$  and reply with:

$$(\vec{c}_0, \vec{c}_1 + \left\lfloor \frac{q}{K} \right\rfloor \cdot \vec{x}_0)$$
, if  $\beta = 0$  and  $(\vec{c}_0, \vec{c}_2 + \left\lfloor \frac{q}{K} \right\rfloor \cdot \vec{x}_1)$ , if  $\beta = 1$ 

If the LWE oracle outputs a uniformly random element, then  $\mathbf{Adv}_{\mathsf{Game}_1}(\mathcal{A}) \leq \mathbf{Adv}_{\mathsf{LWE}_{q,\alpha,n}}(\mathcal{A})$  and the view is identical to  $\mathsf{Game}_1$ . When the LWE oracle outputs an element of the form  $\vec{c} = \mathbf{B}\vec{d} + \vec{g}$ , with  $\vec{d} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_q^n$  and  $\vec{g} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^{m+2\ell}}$ , the split into  $(\vec{c}_0, \vec{c}_1, \vec{c}_2) \in \mathbb{Z}_q^m \times (\mathbb{Z}_q^\ell)^2$  produces ciphertexts formed exactly as in  $\mathsf{Game}_0$ .

We are left to argue that  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}_0^*}, \mathbf{U}_{\mathsf{id}_1^*})$  in Game<sub>1</sub> is distributed statistically close as in Game<sub>2</sub>, which is the case by Lemma 4.6.

We have thus proven that:

$$\mathbf{Adv}_{\text{IND-FE-CPA}}(\mathcal{A}) \leq \tfrac{1}{Q^2} \max(\mathbf{Adv}_{\mathsf{LWE}_{q,\alpha,n}}(\mathcal{A}), \mathbf{Adv}_{\mathsf{LWE}_{q,\sigma/q,n}}(\mathcal{A})) \leq \tfrac{1}{Q^2} \mathbf{Adv}_{\mathsf{LWE}_{q,\alpha,n}}(\mathcal{A}),$$

where Q is the number of random-oracle queries.

## <span id="page-28-0"></span>5 A Lattice-Based Identity-Based Functional Encryption in the Standard Model

In this section, we present a lattice-based IFE construction for the inner-product functionality in the standard model. However, before doing so, we first recall some known results that we are going to use in our construction and security proofs.

## 5.1 Preliminaries

<span id="page-28-1"></span>**Lemma 5.1** ([BGG<sup>+</sup>14]). Let n, m, q > 0 be integers, where q prime. There exist polynomial time algorithms, such that:

{29}------------------------------------------------

- <span id="page-29-7"></span>- TrapGen $(1^n, 1^m, q) \to (\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{T_A})$  ([Ajt99, AP09, MP12]): a PPT algorithm that, for  $m = \Theta(n \log q)$ , outputs a full-rank  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times m}$  and a basis  $\mathbf{T_A} \in \mathbb{Z}^{m \times m}$  for  $\Lambda_q^{\perp}(\mathbf{A})$ . In addition,  $\mathbf{A}$  is  $\operatorname{negl}(n)$ -close to uniform and  $\|\widetilde{\mathbf{T_A}}\| = O(\sqrt{n \log q})$ , with overwhelming probability in n.
- ExtendRight( $\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{T_A}, \mathbf{B}$ )  $\to \mathbf{T_{(A|B)}}$  ([CHKP10]): a deterministic algorithm that for full-rank  $\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{B} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times m}$  and a basis  $\mathbf{T_A} \in \mathbb{Z}^{m \times m}$  of  $\Lambda_a^{\perp}(\mathbf{A})$ , outputs a basis  $\mathbf{T_{(A|B)}}$  of  $\Lambda_a^{\perp}(\mathbf{A}|\mathbf{B})$ . In addition  $\|\widetilde{\mathbf{T_A}}\| = \|\widetilde{\mathbf{T_{(A|B)}}}\|$ .
- basis  $\mathbf{T_A} \in \mathbb{Z}^{m \times m}$  of  $\Lambda_q^{\perp}(\mathbf{A})$ , outputs a basis  $\mathbf{T_{(A|B)}}$  of  $\Lambda_q^{\perp}(\mathbf{A}|\mathbf{B})$ . In addition  $\|\widetilde{\mathbf{T}_A}\| = \|\widetilde{\mathbf{T}_{(A|B)}}\|$ .

   ExtendLeft $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{G}, \mathbf{T_G}, \mathbf{S}) \to \mathbf{T_{(A|G+AS)}}$  ([ABB10]): a deterministic algorithm that for full-rank  $\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{G} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times m}$  and a basis  $\mathbf{T_G}$  of  $\Lambda_q^{\perp}(\mathbf{G})$ , outputs a basis  $\mathbf{T_{(A|G+AS)}}$  of  $\Lambda_q^{\perp}(\mathbf{A}|\mathbf{G}+\mathbf{AS})$ . In addition  $\|\widetilde{\mathbf{T}_{(A|G+AS)}}\| \leq \|\widetilde{\mathbf{T}_G}\|(1+\|\mathbf{S}\|_2)$ .
- For m=n, there exists a full-rank gadget matrix  $\mathbf{G}\in\mathbb{Z}_q^{n\times m}$  for which  $\Lambda_q^\perp(\mathbf{G})$  has a publicly known basis  $\mathbf{T}_{\mathbf{G}}$ , where  $\|\widetilde{\mathbf{T}}_{\mathbf{G}}\|\leq\sqrt{5}$

<span id="page-29-1"></span>**Theorem 5.2 (SampleLeft [ABB10]).** Let q>2, full rank  $\mathbf{A},\mathbf{B}\in\mathbb{Z}_q^{n\times m}$  with m>n, a basis  $\mathbf{T}_{\mathbf{A}}$  of  $\Lambda_q^{\perp}(\mathbf{A})$ , a matrix  $\mathbf{U}\in\mathbb{Z}_q^{n\times\ell}$  and  $\sigma>\|\widetilde{\mathbf{T}}_{\mathbf{A}}\|\cdot\omega(\sqrt{\log m})$ . Then there exists PPT algorithm SampleLeft $(\mathbf{A},\mathbf{T}_{\mathbf{A}},\mathbf{B},\mathbf{U},\sigma)$  that outputs a matrix  $\mathbf{X}\in\mathbb{Z}_q^{2m\times\ell}$ , distributed statistically close to  $D_{\Lambda_q^{\vec{u}}(\mathbf{A}|\mathbf{B}),\sigma}$ .

<span id="page-29-6"></span>**Theorem 5.3 (SampleRight [ABB10, CHKP10]).** Let q > 2, full rank  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times m}$  with m > n, matrices  $\mathbf{S} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{m \times m}$ ,  $\mathbf{U} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times \ell}$ ,  $y \neq 0 \in \mathbb{Z}_q$  and  $\sigma = \sqrt{5} \cdot (1 + \|\mathbf{S}\|_2) \cdot \omega(\sqrt{\log m})$ . Then there exists PPT algorithm SampleRight( $\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{S}, y, \mathbf{U}, \sigma$ ) that outputs a matrix  $\mathbf{X} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{2m \times \ell}$ , distributed statistically close to  $D_{\Lambda_q^{\vec{u}}(\mathbf{A}|\mathbf{AS} + y\mathbf{G}), \sigma}$ , where  $\mathbf{G}$  is the gadget matrix from Lemma 5.1.

<span id="page-29-2"></span>**Lemma 5.4** (Bounding the Norm of a  $\{\pm 1\}^{k \times m}$  Matrix [ABB10]). Let R be a matrix chosen uniformly at random from  $\{\pm 1\}^{k \times m}$ . There exists a universal constant C', for which:

$$\Pr[\|\mathbf{R}\| \ge C'\sqrt{k+m}] < \frac{1}{e^{k+m}}.$$

<span id="page-29-4"></span>**Lemma 5.5** ([Cai98] Gram-Schmidt Minimum). For any arbitrary integer lattice  $\Lambda$ , it holds that:

<span id="page-29-0"></span>
$$1 \le \min_{\mathbf{B}} \|\widetilde{\mathbf{B}}\| \le \lambda_1(\Lambda^*) \cdot O(n),$$

with the minimum is over all (ordered) bases **B** of lattice  $\Lambda$ .

<span id="page-29-5"></span>**Lemma 5.6** ([ABB10]). Let q prime and  $m > (n+1)\log q + \omega(\log n)$ . Let  $\mathbf{S}$  be chosen uniformly from  $\{\pm 1\}^{m\times k}$  mod q, with k polynomial in n. Let  $\mathbf{A}$ ,  $\mathbf{B}$  be chosen uniformly from  $\mathbb{Z}_q^{n\times m}$  and  $\mathbb{Z}_q^{n\times k}$ . Then, for every  $\vec{e} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^m$ , we have that the distributions  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{AS}, \mathbf{S}^{\mathsf{T}}\vec{e})$  and  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{B}, \mathbf{S}^{\mathsf{T}}\vec{e})$  are statistically close.

To encode identities, we need to recall the following definition from [ABB10]:

**Definition 5.7** ([ABB10] Encoding Identities as Matrices). Let q be a prime and n a positive integer. We say that a function  $H: \mathbb{Z}_q^n \to \mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times n}$  is an encoding with full-rank differences when:

- for all distinct  $u, v \in \mathbb{Z}_q^n$ , the matrix  $H(u) H(v) \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times n}$  has full-rank
- H must also be computable in polynomial time in  $n \log q$ .

A scheme for encodings with full-rank differences has been introduced by [ABB10].

<span id="page-29-3"></span>**Definition 5.8 (Identity-Based Encryption).** An Identity-Based Encryption scheme is a tuple of four algorithms (Setup, Extract, Encrypt, Decrypt). The first algorithm, Setup produces some public parameters pk and a master secret key pk mk. Extract(pk, id) generates private keys pk skid, associated to any given identity id. Encrypt produces encryptions under a target identity id using the public parameters pk, and Decrypt will take as input a key pk skid and a ciphertext pk of some message pk it will recover the initial message pk if pk if pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in pk in

<span id="page-30-0"></span>**Definition 5.9 (Indistinguishability from Random).** This security notion for IBE implies both indistinguishability and receiver anonymity (meaning that for every id, without  $sk_{id}$ , one is not able to determine whether any ciphertext is encrypted under id). Moreover, any ciphertext should be indistinguishable from a uniformly random element from the ciphertext space. More formally, consider the following security game for any stateful PPT adversary A, and every security parameter  $\lambda$ , and  $\beta \in \{0,1\}$ :

where C is the ciphertext space. Extract(·) is an oracle that on input id, outputs Extract(mk, id), only if id  $\neq$  id\*.

An identity encryption scheme is INDr-sID-CPA-secure if for every PPT adversary. A the following advanta
