#### 5.2 Our Construction

In this section, we combine the construction idea showcased in Section 4 with the standard-model IBE of [ABB10]. Similarly to the random-oracle model, we prove the resulting scheme anonymous, a property inherited by the underlying IBE scheme [ABB10] we build on. What we obtain is a scheme which is selectively secure with respect to the identities  $id_0^*$ ,  $id_1^*$  used for the challenges  $\vec{x}_0$  and  $\vec{x}_1$ . Since the scheme will make use once again of ALS as a building block, the security will be adaptive with respect to the challenge vectors  $\vec{x}_0$ ,  $\vec{x}_1$  themselves. The construction is described in Fig. 12. From our choice of q, this error is small enough to allow correct decryption.

**Parameters** Recall that plaintext vectors  $\vec{x} \in \mathcal{X} = \{0, \dots, P-1\}$ , decryption key vectors  $\vec{y} \in \mathcal{Y} = \{0, \dots, V-1\}$  and when identities match, we recover  $\langle \vec{x}, \vec{y} \rangle \in \{0, \dots, K-1\}$  with  $K = \ell PV$ . A first-time reader may want to skip this paragraph, as it references steps of the proof. Combining the ALS requirements from Appendix A with the parameters from [ABB10]. our scheme parameters must satisfy the following:

- We start with ALS parameters  $n_{ALS}$  (the security parameter),  $q_{ALS}$ ,  $\sigma_{ALS}$ ,  $\rho_{ALS}$ ,  $m_{ALS} = 2n_{ALS} \log q$ ,  $n = n_{ALS}$ ,  $\alpha_{ALS}$ , chosen as in Appendix A.
- From the ALS reduction in Game<sub>5</sub>,  $\sigma = \sigma_{ALS}$ ,  $q = q_{ALS}$ ,  $n = n_{ALS}$ .  $m = m_{ALS}$ ,  $\rho = \rho_{ALS}$ .
- SampleLeft and SampleRight, we require that  $\rho > m \cdot \omega(\sqrt{\log m})$ .
- TrapGen requires that  $m > 6n \log q$ .
- Leftover hash lemma, this carries over from ALS parameters.
- Guessing  $T_A$  step:  $\rho > n \cdot \omega(\sqrt{n})$ . Notice that increasing  $\rho_{ALS}$  can be done without invalidating LWE security. We only need to increase  $q_{ALS}$  to satisfy correctness.
- Hardness of LWE:  $\alpha q > 2\sqrt{n}$ .
- NoiseGen: The spectral norm of  $S^*$  can be upper-bounded (by using the Frobenius norm) by  $s_1(S^*) \leq m$ . Using Lemma 4.1,  $s_1(\mathbf{Z}_2) \leq C \cdot \rho(2\sqrt{n} + \sqrt{m})$ , which implies that  $\tau \geq C \cdot m\rho(2\sqrt{n} + \sqrt{m})$ .
- Correctness:  $q > 2K\ell V(\sigma + \tau) + C'\sigma\rho 4m\sqrt{\ell nm}V$ .

Therefore, we choose ALS parameters, and then modify the following to satisfy our additional constraints:

- $m \ge 6n \log q$ .
- q is now  $q > 2K\ell V(\sigma + \tau) + C'\sigma\rho 4m\sqrt{\ell nm}V$ .

{31}------------------------------------------------

```
Setup(1^{\lambda}, \mathcal{X}, \mathcal{Y}):
                                                                                                                                                                                             Enc(mpk, id, \vec{x}):
\overline{(\mathbf{A},\mathbf{T}_{\mathbf{A}})\leftarrow_{\scriptscriptstyle{\mathbf{R}}}\mathsf{Tra}}\mathsf{pGen}(1^n,1^m)
                                                                                                                                                                                             \mathbf{H}_{\mathsf{id}} \leftarrow (\mathbf{A}|\mathbf{B} + H(\mathsf{id}) \cdot \mathbf{G}) \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times 2m}
\mathbf{B}, \mathbf{D} \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times m}
                                                                                                                                                                                             \vec{s} \leftarrow_{\mathbf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_q^n
                                                                                                                                                                                            \mathbf{S} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \{ \stackrel{\mathsf{d}}{\pm} 1 \}^{m \times m}
\mathsf{mpk} \leftarrow (\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{B}, \mathbf{D})
\mathsf{msk} \leftarrow \mathbf{T_A}
                                                                                                                                                                                             \vec{e}_1 \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^{2m},\sigma}
Return (mpk, msk)
                                                                                                                                                                                            |\vec{e}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^n,\sigma}|
                                                                                                                                                                                            |\vec{e}_3 \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^n, \tau}
KeyGen(id, \vec{y}):
                                                                                                                                                                                            |\vec{f} \leftarrow (\mathbf{I}_m | \mathbf{S})^{\!\top} \cdot \vec{e}_1
\mathbf{H}_{\mathsf{id}} \leftarrow (\mathbf{A}|\mathbf{B} + H(\mathsf{id}) \cdot \mathbf{G})
                                                                                                                                                                                            \left|\mathsf{ct}_1 \leftarrow \mathbf{H}_\mathsf{id}^{\!\top} \vec{s} + \vec{f}\right|
\mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{SampleLeft}(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{T_A}, \mathbf{B} + H(\mathsf{id}) \cdot \mathbf{G}, \mathbf{D}, \rho)
                                                                                                                                                                                           \left| \mathsf{ct}_2 = \mathbf{D}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{s} + \vec{e}_2 + \vec{e}_3 + \left| \frac{q}{K} \right| \cdot \vec{x} \right|
Return (\vec{y}, \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{id}, \vec{y}} := (\mathbf{R}_{id} \cdot \vec{y}))
                                                                                                                                                                                              Return (\mathsf{ct}_1, \mathsf{ct}_2)
                                                                                                                                                                                            |\mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{ct}_1,\mathsf{ct}_2,\mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{id},\vec{y}},\vec{y}):
                                                                                                                                                                                           |\overline{\mu = \vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle\top} \cdot \mathsf{ct}_2 - \mathsf{sk}^{\scriptscriptstyle\top}_{\mathsf{id},\vec{y}} \cdot \mathsf{ct}_1}|
                                                                                                                                                                                           \left| \mu' = \arg\min_{\mu' \in \{0...K+1\}} \left| \left| \frac{q}{K} \right| \cdot \mu - \mu' \right| \right|
                                                                                                                                                                                            Return \mu'
```

<span id="page-31-0"></span>**Fig. 12.** An identity-based inner-product functional encryption scheme  $\mathcal{IFE}$ . Function H is an encoding with full-rank difference, as in Definition 5.7. Algorithms TrapGen and SampleLeft are described in Lemma 5.1 and Theorem 5.2. Noise  $\vec{e}_3$  is non-smudging and is of a different standard deviation  $\tau$ , this is needed for the security proof.

• 
$$\tau \geq C \cdot m \rho (2\sqrt{n} + \sqrt{m}).$$

**Lemma 5.10** (Decryption correctness). For parameters  $n, m, q, \sigma, \rho, \tau$ , chosen as in the previous paragraph, the scheme from Fig. 11 is correct.

*Proof.*  $\mu = \vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \cdot \mathsf{ct}_2 - \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{id},\vec{y}}^{\mathsf{T}} \cdot \mathsf{ct}_1 = \vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{D}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{s} + \vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{e}_2 + \vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{e}_3 + \left\lfloor \frac{q}{K} \right\rfloor \langle \vec{x}, \vec{y} \rangle - \vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{H}_{\mathsf{id}}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{s} - \vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{f}$ . We know that  $\mathbf{H}_{\mathsf{id}} \mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}} = \mathbf{D}$ , therefore the expression above can be rewritten as:

$$\mu = \left\lfloor \frac{q}{K} \right\rfloor \langle \vec{x}, \vec{y} \rangle + \underbrace{\vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{e}_2 + \vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{e}_3 - \vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{f}}_{\mathsf{error terms}}$$

By Lemma 4.2, we now bound the error terms, by rewritting them as:  $\vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \vec{e}_2 + \vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \vec{e}_3 - \vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \vec{f} = \vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \vec{e}_2 + \vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \vec{e}_3 - \vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \vec{f} = \vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \vec{e}_2 + \vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \vec{e}_3 - \vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} (\mathbf{I}_m | \mathbf{S})^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \vec{e}_1$ . Since  $\mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{2m \times n}$ , we know that  $\|\mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}}\| \leq \rho \sqrt{2mn}$ . Also  $\|\mathbf{S}\| \leq C \sqrt{2m}$ ,  $\|\vec{e}_1\| \leq \sigma \sqrt{2m}$ ,  $\|\vec{e}_1\| \leq \sigma \sqrt{2m}$ ,  $\|\vec{e}_2\| \leq \sigma \sqrt{2m}$ , therefore  $\|\vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \vec{e}_3\| \leq \tau \ell V$  and  $\|\vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}}^{\scriptscriptstyle T} (\mathbf{I}_m | \mathbf{S})^{\scriptscriptstyle T} \vec{e}_1\| \leq C' \sigma \rho 4m \sqrt{\ell nm} V$ , where the last inequality is derived from Lemma 5.4. Therefore, the final error term is upper bounded by  $\ell V(\sigma + \tau) + C' \sigma \rho 4m \sqrt{\ell nm} V$ .

<span id="page-31-1"></span>Remark 5.11 (Inheriting Anonymity). Towards proving security, we recall the observation made during the proof of our random-oracle construction. Assume that  $\mathrm{id}_0^* \neq \mathrm{id}_1^*$  - if the adversary was allowed to obtain secret keys for either  $\mathrm{id}_0^* \| \vec{y}$  or  $\mathrm{id}_1^* \| \vec{y}$ , for any  $\vec{y}$ , then it could trivially distinguish between encryptions of  $\vec{x}_0$  under  $\mathrm{id}_0^*$  and encryptions of  $\vec{x}_1$  under  $\mathrm{id}_1^*$ . This type of trivial attack is excluded by the SEL-IND definition, therefore we conclude that the adversary cannot obtain decryption key queries for neither  $\mathrm{id}_0^*$  or  $\mathrm{id}_1^*$ . Then, there are no functional keys and the adversary interacts with what is essentially an identity-based encryption scheme (in our case, exactly the scheme of [ABB10]). Therefore, since [ABB10] satisfies INDr-sID-CPA (see Definition 5.9), our scheme will trivially also satisfy the same security notion.

We can now state the following security theorem:

**Theorem 5.12 (SEL-IND Security).** Let n be the security parameter and consider the ALS functional encryption scheme (Appendix A) with parameters q,  $\sigma$ ,  $\rho$ , m, n,  $\alpha$ . As long as ALS is secure under the LWE $_{q,\alpha,n}$  and the ALS parameters satisfy in addition  $m \geq 6n \log q$ ,  $q > 2K\ell V(\sigma + \tau) + C'\sigma \rho 4m\sqrt{\ell nm}V$ , the scheme in Fig. 12 with  $\tau \geq C \cdot m\rho(2\sqrt{n} + \sqrt{m})$  is SEL-IND secure under the LWE $_{q,\alpha,n}$  assumption.

{32}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-32-0"></span>*Proof.* Since the case of  $id_0^* \neq id_1^*$  follows from Remark 5.11, we focus on the more difficult case when  $id^* = id_0^* = id_1^*$ . The proof follows the outline of the original [ABB10]. The novelty is the adaptation for supporting functional decryption keys. An additional step we did not encounter in the random oracle construction is that here, when matrix **A** is switched from its trapdoored version to uniformly random, the simulator still needs to have access to a short basis in order to answer key decryption queries for the case  $id^*$ . This is a difference from the proof of [ABB10], where they do not need to answer any queries for the punctured identity  $id^*$ . Since our proof is selective with respect to the identity of the challenges, we assume that we have access to  $id^*$ . We also make use of noise rerandomization and correction techniques from [KY16], which is why we require an additional non-smudging noise  $\vec{e}_3$ .

**Game 0** This is the original SEL-IND game, the challenge plaintexts are  $(\vec{x}_0, \vec{x}_1)$ .

**Game 1** Instead of choosing  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{T}) \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathsf{TrapGen}(1^n, 1^m)$ , choose  $\mathbf{A}$  to be uniformly random. To compute decryption keys, we can enumerate all short possible basis  $\mathbf{T}_{\mathbf{A}}$  of  $\Lambda_q^{\perp}(\mathbf{A})$  and use one of these short basis to generate decryption keys. This game is inefficient and indistinguishable from the previous one by the properties of  $\mathsf{TrapGen}()$  (see Lemma 5.1). Since the indistinguishability is derived from a statistical argument, it is not problematic that this game is inefficient.

However, we need to ensure that the lattice spanned by  $\bf A$  actually has a short basis. For this, we use Lemma 5.5, which says that:  $\min_{\bf B} \|\widetilde{\bf B}\| \le O(n)$ , which means that in order to use the SampleD algorithm, we need to set our standard deviation  $\rho > n \cdot \omega(\sqrt{n})$ . This will require us to increase the standard deviation  $\rho$  used in SampleLeft, which in turn will lead to a larger modulus than in our random oracle scheme.

Game 2 In this game, we switch the computation of  $\mathbf{D}$  to be programmed to  $\mathbf{D} = \mathbf{H}_{\mathsf{id}^*} \mathbf{Z}$ , where  $\mathbf{Z} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} D_{\mathbb{Z}^{2m \times n}, \rho}$ , using SampleD. We now justify that this change is (statistically) indistinguishable for the adversary. Recall that  $\mathbf{H}_{\mathsf{id}^*} = (\mathbf{A}|\mathbf{B} + H(\mathsf{id}^*)\mathbf{G})$ . Since  $\mathbf{B}$  and  $\mathbf{A}$  are uniformly random, we can apply Lemma 4.6 and argue that  $\mathbf{H}_{\mathsf{id}^*}\mathbf{Z} = (\mathbf{A}|\mathbf{B} + H(\mathsf{id}^*)\mathbf{G})\mathbf{Z}$  is close to uniformly random over  $\mathbb{Z}^{n \times \ell}$  (which is how  $\mathbf{D}$  was generated in  $\mathsf{Game}_1$ ).

The role of  $\mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}^*}$  is played by  $\mathbf{Z}$ . Use  $\mathbf{Z}$  to answer decryption key queries for  $\mathsf{id} = \mathsf{id}^*$ , as  $\mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{id}^* \parallel \vec{y}} = \mathbf{Z} \cdot y$ . For all other decryption key queries, we still compute  $\mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{SampleLeft}(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{T}_{\mathbf{A}}, \mathbf{B} + H(\mathsf{id})\mathbf{G}, \mathbf{D})$  and output  $\mathbf{R}_{\mathsf{id}^*} \vec{y}$ 

**Game 3** Matrix  $T_A$  must still be found by enumeration. The matrix  $S^*$  which will be chosen for the challenge ciphertext is now picked at ley generation, just as in [ABB10]. **B** is now chosen as  $B = AS^* - H(id^*)G$ .  $\vec{f} := (I_m|S)^\top \vec{e_1}$ . By Lemma 5.6, we know that if **A** is uniform over  $\mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times m}$  and **S** uniform over  $\{\pm 1\}^{m \times m}$ , the distribution  $(A, B, (S^*)^\top \vec{e_1})$  is statistically close to  $(A, AS^*, (S^*)^\top \vec{e_1})$ , where **B** is uniform over  $\mathbb{Z}_q^{n \times m}$ . Note that the distributions are close for any choice of  $\vec{e}$ , so  $\vec{e}$  can be known to a distinguishing adversary in this step.

Game 4 This game is now efficient. Use SampleRight( $\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{S}, H(\mathrm{id}) - H(\mathrm{id}^*), \mathbf{D}, \rho$ ) with standard deviation  $\rho$  to generate  $\mathbf{R}_{\mathrm{id}}$ , for id  $\neq$  id\*. This is possible due to the identity encoding function (Definition 5.7), which ensures that  $H(\mathrm{id}) - H(\mathrm{id}^*)$  is non-zero, one of the requirements of the SampleRight algorithm. For id = id\*, the decryption keys are generated using the secret matrix  $\mathbf{Z}$ . This game is indistinguishable from the previous game due to Theorem 5.2, which says that the computed  $\mathbf{Z}_{\mathrm{id}}$  is statistically close to  $D_{\Lambda_q^{\mathbf{U}}(\mathbf{A}|\mathbf{AS}+(H(\mathrm{id})-H(\mathrm{id}^*)\mathbf{G}),\sigma}$ . This holds as long as the standard deviation  $\rho$  satisfies the constraints of Theorem 5.3.

**Game 5** In this final game, we rely on the security of ALS to argue indistinguishability of ciphertexts. We interact with the AD-CPA challenger for ALS. We receive as public keys  $A_{ALS}$  and  $D_{ALS}$ . We simulate the view of the adversary in the following manner:

- Setup: set  $\mathbf{A} := \mathbf{A}_{ALS}^{\mathsf{T}}$ , pick  $\mathbf{Z}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^{n \times m}, \sigma}, \mathbf{S}^* \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \{\pm 1\}^{m \times m}$  and set  $\mathbf{D} = \mathbf{D}_{ALS} + \mathbf{A}\mathbf{S}^*\mathbf{Z}_2$ .
- **Decryption keys for** id\* $\|\tilde{\mathbf{y}}$ : Ask the ALS functional key oracle for key  $\vec{y}$ , obtain  $\mathsf{sk}_{\vec{y}}$ , return  $\mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{id}^* \| \vec{y}} = \left(\frac{\mathsf{sk}_{\vec{y}}}{\mathbf{Z}_2 \vec{y}}\right)$ , which we can compute since we know  $\mathbf{Z}_2$ .

{33}------------------------------------------------

- **Decryption keys for** id  $\neq$  id\*: Using SampleRight as in previous games.
- Challenge ciphertext upon receiving  $\vec{x}_0, \vec{x}_1$  from the adversary, we forward them to the ALS challenger and receive  $(\mathsf{ct}_1^{\mathsf{ALS}}, \mathsf{ct}_2^{\mathsf{ALS}})$ . Then, compute and return:

$$\begin{aligned} \mathsf{ct}_1 &= \mathsf{ct}_1^{\mathsf{ALS}} + (\mathbf{S}^*)^{\!\top} \mathsf{ct}_1^{\mathsf{ALS}} \\ \\ \mathsf{ct}_2 &= \mathsf{ct}_2^{\mathsf{ALS}} + \mathbf{Z}_2^{\!\top} (\mathbf{S}^*)^{\!\top} \mathsf{ct}_1^{\mathsf{ALS}} + \mathsf{NoiseGen}(\mathbf{Z}_2^{\!\top} (\mathbf{S}^*)^{\!\top}, s) \end{aligned}$$

In this game the advantage of the adversary is upper bounded by the advantage of breaking the ALS scheme. It remains to show that  $Game_5$  is indistinguishable from  $Game_4$ . We start by splitting  $\mathbf{R}_{id^*} := \left(\frac{\mathbf{Z}_1}{\mathbf{Z}_2}\right)$ . Then the fact that  $(\mathbf{A}\|\mathbf{A}\mathbf{S}^*)\mathbf{R}_{id^*} = \mathbf{D}$  translates to the fact that  $\mathbf{A}\mathbf{Z}_1 + \mathbf{A}\mathbf{S}^*\mathbf{Z}_2 = \mathbf{D}$ . The second matrix  $\mathbf{Z}_2$  we can simply sample from  $\mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^n \times m, \rho, c}$ , while  $\mathbf{S}^*$  is known and  $\mathbf{D}$  is programmable (since  $Game_2$ ). Now we set  $\mathbf{A}^\top = \mathbf{A}_{ALS}$  and implicitly  $\mathbf{Z}_1^\top \mathbf{A}^\top = \mathbf{D}_{ALS}$  (Recall that  $\mathbf{Z}_1$  is the master secret key of ALS, so we do not have access to it). Notice that:

$$\mathsf{ct}_1 = (\mathbf{I}_m | \mathbf{S}^*)^{\mathsf{T}} (\mathbf{A}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{s} + \vec{e}_1) = (\mathbf{A} | \mathbf{A} \mathbf{S}^*)^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{s} + (\mathbf{I}_m | \mathbf{S}^*)^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{e}_1$$

$$\mathsf{ct}_2 = \mathbf{D}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{s} + \left\lfloor \frac{q}{K} \right\rfloor \cdot \vec{x} + \vec{e}_2 = \mathbf{Z}_1^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{A}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{s} + \mathbf{Z}_2^{\mathsf{T}} (\mathbf{S}^*)^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{A}^{\mathsf{T}} \vec{s} + \left\lfloor \frac{q}{K} \right\rfloor \cdot \vec{x}^{\beta}$$

Let  $e_1$  be the noise of the first ALS ciphertext. Then we can rewrite to:

$$\mathsf{ct}_1 = \mathsf{ct}_1^{\mathsf{ALS}} + (\mathbf{S}^*)^{\!\top} \mathsf{ct}_1^{\mathsf{ALS}}$$

$$\mathsf{ct}_2 = \mathbf{D}^{\!\scriptscriptstyle \top} \vec{s} + \left| \frac{q}{K} \right| \cdot \vec{x} + \vec{e}_2 + \vec{e}_3 = \mathsf{ct}_2^{\mathsf{ALS}} + \mathbf{Z}_2^{\!\scriptscriptstyle \top} (\mathbf{S}^*)^{\!\scriptscriptstyle \top} \mathsf{ct}_1^{\mathsf{ALS}} + \mathsf{NoiseGen}(\mathbf{Z}_2^{\!\scriptscriptstyle \top} (\mathbf{S}^*)^{\!\scriptscriptstyle \top}, s)$$

As already explained in our parameter section, we can upper-bound  $s_1(\mathbf{Z}_2^{\mathsf{T}}(\mathbf{S}^*))$  and we let  $s > s_1(\mathbf{Z}_2^{\mathsf{T}}(\mathbf{S}^*))$ . To conclude, by the properties of NoiseGen $(\mathbf{Z}_2^{\mathsf{T}}(\mathbf{S}^*)^{\mathsf{T}}, s)$ , we know that the noise in ct<sub>2</sub> will be statistically close to  $\vec{e}_2 + \vec{e}_3$ , therefore Game<sub>5</sub> is statistically close to Game<sub>4</sub>.

## <span id="page-33-0"></span>6 Multi-Input Inner-Product Functional Encryption with Rich Access Control

In this section, we build a multi-input FE scheme for the family of functions  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{multi}}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d,B),\mathsf{P}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{P}_n}$  for a dimension  $d \in \mathbb{N}$ , a bound  $B \in \mathbb{N}$ , and n predicates  $\mathsf{P}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{P}_n \in \mathcal{P}$ , where each predicate takes as input an attribute in the universe  $\mathcal{U}$ , and returns a bit. Each function is described by a set of indices  $\mathcal{S} \subseteq [n]$ , and for each index  $\in \mathcal{S}$ , an associated pair (att<sub>i</sub>  $\in \mathcal{U}, \vec{y}_i \in [0,B]^d$ ). It takes as input n vectors  $\vec{x}_1,\ldots,\vec{x}_n \in [0,B]^d$ , and outputs  $\sum_{i\in\mathcal{S}} \vec{x}_i^\top \vec{y}_i \in [0,ndB^2]$  if  $\mathsf{P}_i(\mathsf{att}_i) = 1$  for all  $i \in \mathcal{S}$ .

Note that to each input slot corresponds to a different predicate  $P_i$ , which represents a different policy access to the encrypted data. For instance, some medical data can be considered very sensitive, and should only be computed on by doctors, whereas other records might be slightly less sensitive, and could pertain to the output of a computation performed by a user with lower credentials, say, a health insurance company. Each functional secret key permits to compute a weighted sum on some part of the data for which it has credentials (this is represented by the set S). That is, a key decrypts successfully provided it is associated with attributes that satisfy the predicates associated with the encrypted data on which it computes.

Our construction generically transforms any single-input FE for the family of functions  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d+1,B),\mathcal{P}}$  (described in Section 3) satisfying some structural properties into an MIFE for the family of functions  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d,B),\mathsf{P}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{P}_n}^{\mathsf{multi}}$ . We first recall the definition of multi-input FE and then describe our construction.

{34}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-34-0"></span>**6.1** Definitions

**Definition 6.1** (Multi-Input Functional Encryption [GGG<sup>+</sup>14]). Let  $\mathcal{F}$  be a family of n-ary functions, with  $f \in \mathcal{F}$  defined as  $f : \mathcal{X}^n \to \mathcal{Y}$ . A functional encryption scheme for  $\mathcal{F}$  consists of the following algorithms:

- Setup( $1^{\lambda}$ ,  $\mathcal{F}$ ): takes as input the security parameter  $\lambda$  and a description of the function family  $\mathcal{F}$ , and outputs a master public key mpk (which is implicitly part of the inputs of all other algorithms), a master secret key msk, and n encryption keys  $ek_1, \ldots, ek_n$ .
- $\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{ek}_i, x_i)$ : takes as input an encryption key  $\mathsf{ek}_i$ , a message  $x_i \in \mathcal{X}$ , and outputs a ciphertext  $\mathsf{ct}_i$ .
- KeyGen(msk, f): takes as input the master secret key msk, a function  $f \in \mathcal{F}$ . It outputs a functional decryption key sk<sub>f</sub>.
- $\operatorname{Dec}(\operatorname{sk}_f,(\operatorname{ct}_1,\ldots,\operatorname{ct}_n))$ : takes as input the functional decryption key  $\operatorname{sk}_f$  along with ciphertexts  $(\operatorname{ct}_1,\ldots,\operatorname{ct}_n)$ . It outputs a value  $y \in \mathcal{Y}$  or the special symbol  $\perp$  if it fails.

A scheme as defined above is correct if for all security parameter  $\lambda$ ,  $f \in \mathcal{F}$ ,  $x_1, \ldots, x_n \in \mathcal{X}$ , we have:  $\Pr\left[\mathsf{Dec}\left(\mathsf{sk}_f, (\mathsf{ct}_1, \ldots, \mathsf{ct}_n)\right) = f(x_1, \ldots, x_n)\right] = 1$  where the probability is taken over  $(\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{msk}, (\mathsf{ek}_1, \ldots, \mathsf{ek}_n)) \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, \mathcal{F})$ ,  $\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{ek}_i, x_i)$  for all  $i \in [n]$ ,  $\mathsf{sk}_f \leftarrow \mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{msk}, f)$ .

**Definition 6.2** (**AD-IND security**). For every functional encryption  $\mathcal{MIFE}$ , every security parameter  $\lambda$ , every stateful adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , we define the following experiments for  $\beta \in \{0, 1\}$ :

where n is the number of users; on input  $i \in [n]$ , the oracle  $\mathsf{OCorrupt}(i)$  returns  $\mathsf{ek}_i$  and adds i to the set  $\mathsf{Corr}$  of corrupted users; on input tuples of the form  $(i, x^0, x^1)$  where  $i \in [n]$ ,  $x^0, x^1 \in \mathcal{X}$ , the oracle  $\mathsf{OEnc}(i, x^0, x^1)$  returns  $\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{ek}_i, x^\beta)$  and adds  $(i, x^0, x^1)$  to the list of queries, denoted by  $\mathcal{Q}$ ; on input a function  $f \in \mathcal{F}$  of arity n, the oracle  $\mathsf{OKeyGen}(f)$  returns the functional decryption key  $\mathsf{sk}_f \leftarrow \mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{msk}, f)$ .

We denote by  $\mathcal I$  the set of pairs  $\left((x_1^0,\dots,x_\ell^0),(x_1^1,\dots,x_\ell^1)\right)$  where for all  $i\in \operatorname{Hon}$ ,  $(i,x_i^0,x_i^1)\in \mathcal Q$  and for all  $i\in \operatorname{Corr}$ ,  $x_i^0=x_i^1\in \mathcal X$ . We require that for all functions f queried to  $\operatorname{OKeyGen}$ , we have:  $f(x_1^0,\dots,x_\ell^0)=f(x_1^1,\dots,x_\ell^1)$  for all pairs  $\left((x_1^0,\dots,x_\ell^0),(x_1^1,\dots,x_\ell^1)\right)\in \mathcal I$ .

Moreover, we require that for all users i that are corrupted during the game, all the queries of the form  $(i, x^0, x^1) \in \mathcal{Q}$  are such that  $x^0 = x^1$ .

Using generic transformations from [ABKW19, Gay19], which build upon [AGRW17, DOT18], we can assume without loss of generality that, if  $i \in \text{Hon}$ , then there exists a query  $(i, x_i^0, x_i^1) \in \mathcal{Q}$ .

A multi-input functional encryption scheme  $\mathcal{FE}$  is AD-IND-secure if for every PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , the following advantage is a negligible function of  $\lambda$ :

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{AD\text{-}IND}}_{\mathcal{FE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) = \big|\Pr\big[\mathbf{AD\text{-}IND}^{\mathcal{FE}}_0(1^\lambda,\mathcal{A}) = 1\big] - \Pr\big[\mathbf{AD\text{-}IND}^{\mathcal{FE}}_1(1^\lambda,\mathcal{A}) = 1\big]\big|$$

We define one-time security with its associated games  $\mathbf{ONE\text{-}AD\text{-}IND}^{\mathcal{FE}}_b$  and advantage  $\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ONE\text{-}AD\text{-}IND}}_{\mathcal{FE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda)$  similarly, except the adversary can make at most one query of the form  $(i,x_i^0,x_i^1)$  for each slot  $i\in[n]$ .

There are weaker security notions, where the set of corrupted users Corr, or the set of queries Q is chosen beforehand by the adversary. The former restriction is referred to as static corruptions, whereas the second restriction is referred to as selective security.

{35}------------------------------------------------

### <span id="page-35-3"></span><span id="page-35-1"></span>**6.2** Generic Construction

We show that the techniques used in [ACF<sup>+</sup>18, AGRW17] to generically transform any single-input FE for inner products into a multi-input FE for inner products are compatible with our single-input FE. Thus, we obtain an MIFE for the family of functions  $\mathcal{F}^{\text{multi}}_{\text{ipfe}(d,B),P_1,...,P_n}$ , described in Fig. 13, which relies on any single-input FE (Setup', Enc', KeyGen', Dec') for the family of functions  $\mathcal{F}_{\text{ipfe}(d+1,B),\mathcal{P}}$ , such as described in Section 3, satisfying the following properties:

- 1. **Pairing-based:** ciphertexts contain groups elements in  $\mathbb{G}_1$  and functional decryption keys contain groups elements in  $\mathbb{G}_2$ , for a pairing group  $\mathcal{PG} = (\mathbb{G}_1, \mathbb{G}_2, \mathbb{G}_T, p, P_1, P_2, e) \leftarrow \mathsf{PGGen}(1^{\lambda})$ , with  $p \gg B$ .
- 2. Large inputs: The algorithms Enc' and KeyGen' can take as input arbitrary vectors in  $\mathbb{Z}_p^d$  (as opposed to vectors in  $[0,B]^d$  for a polynomial bound B) and the decryption returns  $[\vec{x}^\top \vec{y}]_T \in \mathbb{G}_T$ .
- 3. **Linear homomorphism:** There is a PPT algorithm Add such that for all  $\vec{x}, \vec{x}' \in \mathbb{Z}_p^d$  and  $P \in \mathcal{P}$ , the following are identically distributed: (Enc'(mpk, P,  $\vec{x}$ ), Enc'(mpk, P,  $\vec{x}$ + $\vec{x}'$ )) and (Enc'(mpk, P,  $\vec{x}$ ), Add(Enc'(mpk, P,  $\vec{x}$ )). That is, Add can produce a fresh encryption of  $\vec{x} + \vec{x}'$  from an encryption of  $\vec{x}$ , for a given predicate  $P \in \mathcal{P}$ .

It is not hard to verify that our scheme in Section 3.1 satisfies these properties.

<span id="page-35-0"></span>**Fig. 13.** MIFE for the family  $\mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{multi}}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d,B),\mathsf{P}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{P}_n}$ , where  $\mathsf{P}_1,\ldots,\mathsf{P}_n\in\mathcal{P}$ . Here,  $\mathcal{FE}':=(\mathsf{Setup}',\mathsf{Enc}',\mathsf{KeyGen}',\mathsf{Dec}')$  is a SEL-IND secure FE for the family  $\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d,B),\mathcal{P}}$ , satisfying the structural properties listed in Section 6.2.

**Correctness.** Let  $S \subseteq [n]$ ,  $\vec{x}_i \in [0,B]^d$  for  $i \in [n]$ ,  $\vec{y}_j \in [0,B]^d$ , att $_j \in \mathcal{U}$  for  $j \in S$ , such that for all  $j \in S$ ,  $\mathsf{P}_j(\mathsf{att}_j) = 1$ . We have, by correctness of the single-input FE, for all  $j \in S$ ,  $[d_j]_T := [\vec{x}_j^\top \vec{y}_j + \vec{u}_j^\top \vec{y}_j]_T$ . Thus,  $[\mathsf{out}]_T := [\sum_{j \in S} \vec{x}_j^\top \vec{y}_j + \vec{u}_j^\top \vec{y}_j]_T - [z]_T = [\sum_{j \in S} \vec{x}_j^\top \vec{y}_j]_T$ , and the decryption can efficiently recover out, since the output belongs to  $[0, ndB^2]$ , which is polynomially bounded.

<span id="page-35-2"></span>**Theorem 6.3 (IND security).** The MIFE presented in Fig. 4 MIFE is AD-IND secure (respectively SEL-IND secure) as long as the underlying single-input FE  $\mathcal{FE}'$  is AD-IND secure (respectively SEL-IND secure). Namely, for any PPT adversary  $\mathcal{A}$ , there exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}$  such that:

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{AD\text{-}IND}}_{\mathcal{MIFE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq n \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{AD\text{-}IND}}_{\mathcal{FE}',\mathcal{B}}(\lambda) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Similarly, for any PPT adversary A, there exists a PPT adversary B such that:

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{SEL}\text{-}\mathsf{IND}}_{\mathcal{MIFE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq n \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{SEL}\text{-}\mathsf{IND}}_{\mathcal{FE}',\mathcal{B}}(\lambda) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

{36}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-36-3"></span>The proof, as in [ACF<sup>+</sup>18, AGRW17], proceeds in two steps, where we first prove perfect one-time security, then boost it to many-time security using the many-time security of the underlying single-input FE. The adaptive and selective settings have very similar proofs, thus, we only give the proof in the adaptive setting, which is the most technical.

*Proof.* First, in Lemma 6.4, we show that for any adversary A, we have:

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ONE-AD-IND}}_{\mathcal{MIFE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) = 0.$$

Next, for all PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{A}$ , we show there exist PPT adversaries  $\mathcal{B}_1$  and  $\mathcal{B}_2$  such that:

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{AD\text{-}IND}}_{\mathcal{MIFE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) \leq \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ONE\text{-}AD\text{-}IND}}_{\mathcal{MIFE},\mathcal{B}_1}(\lambda) + n \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{AD\text{-}IND}}_{\mathcal{FE}',\mathcal{B}_2}(\lambda) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

The proof uses a series of hybrid games, described in Fig. 14. For game  $G_j$  for all  $j \in [1,3]$ , we denote by  $Adv_i(A)$  the probability that game  $G_i$  outputs 1 when interacting with A. Here, for any input slot  $i \in [n]$ , we denote by  $(i, \vec{x}_i^{j,0}, \vec{x}_i^{j,1})$  the j-th query for slot  $i \in [n]$  (note that there can be many such queries for each slot). It is clear that games  $\mathsf{G}_1$  and  $\mathsf{G}_3$  correspond to  $\mathbf{AD\text{-}IND}_0^{\mathcal{FE}}(1^{\lambda}, \mathcal{A})$  and  $\mathbf{AD\text{-}IND}_1^{\mathcal{FE}}(1^{\lambda}, \mathcal{A})$ , respectively. We show

in Lemma 6.5 that there exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_1$  such that

$$|\mathsf{Adv}_1(\mathcal{A}) - \mathsf{Adv}_2(\mathcal{A})| \leq \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{MIFE},\mathcal{B}_1}^{\mathsf{ONE-AD-IND}}(\lambda).$$

We show in Lemma 6.6 that there exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_2$  such that

$$|\mathsf{Adv}_2(\mathcal{A}) - \mathsf{Adv}_3(\mathcal{A})| \leq n \cdot \mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{AD-IND}}_{\mathcal{FE}',\mathcal{B}_2}(\lambda) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

#### <span id="page-36-0"></span>**Lemma 6.4.** For any adversary A, we have:

$$\mathsf{Adv}^{\mathsf{ONE-AD-IND}}_{\mathcal{MIFE},\mathcal{A}}(\lambda) = 0.$$

*Proof.* We first define a game  $G_0$ , which samples a random bit  $\beta \leftarrow_R \{0,1\}$ , and a guess of all the queries  $(\vec{w}_i^0, \vec{w}_i^1) \leftarrow_R \{0,1\}$  $([0,B]^d)^2$  for all  $i \in [n]$ . Then the experiment behaves as  $\mathbf{AD\text{-}IND}^{\mathcal{FE}}_{\beta}(1^{\lambda},\mathcal{A})$ . At the end of the experiment, the adversary A outputs a bit  $\beta'$ . If the guess was successful, that is, the queries made by A were predicted correctly, with  $\vec{w}_i^0 = \vec{x}_i^0$  and  $\vec{w}_i^1 = \vec{x}_i^1$ , then the experiment outputs  $\beta'$ . Otherwise, it outputs a random bit  $\beta' \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \{0,1\}$ . We define the advantage  $\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{G}_0}(\lambda) := 2 \cdot |1/2 - \Pr[\beta' = \beta | \beta' \leftarrow \mathsf{G}_0]|$ . We have:  $\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{G}_0}(\lambda) = B^{2dn} \cdot \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{MIFE},\mathcal{A}}^{\mathsf{ONE-AD-IND}}(\lambda)$ .

Now, we show that  $\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathsf{G}_0}(\lambda) = 0$ , which implies that  $\mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{MIFE},\mathcal{A}}^{\mathsf{ONE-AD-IND}}(\lambda) = 0$ . We use the fact that  $\{\vec{u}_i\}_{i \in [n]}$ , is

identically distributed to  $\{\vec{u}_i + \vec{w}_i^1 - \vec{w}_i^0\}_{i \in [n]}$ , where  $\vec{u}_i, \vec{w}_i^0, \vec{w}_i^1 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^d$ . When the guess is successful, that is, when  $\vec{w}_i^0 = \vec{x}_i^0$  and  $\vec{w}_i^1 = \vec{x}_i^1$  for all  $i \in [n]$ , the first distribution corresponds to case where b=0 in the experiment  $G_0$ , with challenge ciphertexts of the form  $\operatorname{Enc}'(\operatorname{mpk}_i, \mathsf{P}_i, \vec{x}_i^0 + \vec{u}_i \bmod p)$ and keys of the form  $(\{\text{KeyGen}'(\text{msk}_i, \text{att}_i, \vec{y}_i)\}_{i \in [n]}, \sum_{i \in [n]} \vec{u}_i^\top \vec{y}_i)$ , whereas the second distribution corresponds to the case where b=1 in the experiment  $G_0$ , with challenge ciphertexts of the form  $\operatorname{Enc}'(\operatorname{mpk}_i, \mathsf{P}_i, \vec{x}_i^0 + (\vec{u}_i + \vec{x}_i^1 - \vec{u}_i))$  $\vec{x}_i^0 \pmod{p} = \mathsf{Enc}'(\mathsf{mpk}_i, \mathsf{P}_i, \vec{x}_i^1 + \vec{u}_i \bmod{p}) \text{ and keys of the form } (\mathcal{S}, \{\mathsf{KeyGen}'(\mathsf{msk}_i, \mathsf{att}_i, \vec{y}_i)\}_{i \in \mathcal{S}}, \sum_{i \in \mathcal{S}} (\vec{u}_i + \vec{x}_i) \pmod{p}$  $\vec{x}_i^1 - \vec{x}_i^0)^{\top} \vec{y}_i = \sum_{i \in \mathcal{S}} \vec{u}_i^{\top} \vec{y}_i$ , where the last equality uses the fact that  $\sum_{i \in \mathcal{S}} \vec{y}_i^{\top} \vec{x}_i^0 = \sum_{i \in \mathcal{S}} \vec{y}_i^{\top} \vec{x}_i^1$ , by definition of the security game.

#### <span id="page-36-1"></span>**Lemma 6.5** (From Game $G_1$ to $G_2$ ). There exists a PPT adversary $\mathcal{B}_1$ such that

$$|\mathsf{Adv}_1(\mathcal{A}) - \mathsf{Adv}_2(\mathcal{A})| \leq \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{MIFE},\mathcal{B}_1}^{\mathsf{ONE-AD-IND}}(\lambda).$$

<span id="page-36-2"></span>*Proof.* Adversary  $\mathcal{B}_1$  receives mpk which it forwards to  $\mathcal{A}$ . When  $\mathcal{A}$  queries OKeyGen or OCorrupt,  $\mathcal{B}$  forwards the query to its own oracle and gives back the answer to  $\mathcal{A}$ . When  $\mathcal{A}$  queries  $\mathsf{OEnc}(i, \vec{x}_i^{1,0}, \vec{x}_x^{1,1})$  on slot  $i \in [n]$ for the first time,  $\mathcal{B}_1$  forwards the query to its own oracle and gives back the answer to  $\mathcal{A}$ , which is of the form  $\mathsf{ct}_i' \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathsf{Enc}'(\mathsf{mpk}_i, \mathsf{P}_i, \vec{x}_i^{1,b} + \vec{u}_i \bmod p)$ . For any subsequent queries  $(i, \vec{x}_i^{j,0}, \vec{x}_x^{j,1})$  to  $\mathsf{OEnc}$ , that is, for j > 1,  $\mathcal{B}_1$ uses the linear homomorphism to compute  $\operatorname{ct}_i \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \operatorname{Add}(\operatorname{ct}_i', \vec{x}_i^{j,0} - \vec{x}_i^{1,0})$ , which by property 3 is distributed as a fresh encryption of the form  $\operatorname{Enc}'(\operatorname{mpk}_i, \mathsf{P}_i, \vec{x}_i^{j,0} + \vec{x}_i^{1,b} - \vec{x}_i^{1,0})$ . Note that in the case b = 0, this corresponds to the game  $G_1$ , whereas it corresponds to the game  $G_2$  when b = 1.

{37}------------------------------------------------

**Lemma 6.6** (From Game  $G_2$  to  $G_3$ ). There exists a PPT adversary  $\mathcal{B}_2$  such that

$$|\mathsf{Adv}_2(\mathcal{A}) - \mathsf{Adv}_3(\mathcal{A})| \leq n \cdot \mathsf{Adv}_{\mathcal{FE}',\mathcal{B}_2}^{\mathsf{AD-IND}}(\lambda) + \mathsf{negl}(\lambda).$$

Proof. We switch the distribution of the challenge ciphertexts output by OEnc, one slot  $i \in [n]$  at a time, using a hybrid argument. To change the ciphertexts for slot  $i \in [n]$ , we use the security of  $\mathcal{FE}'$  on  $(\mathsf{mpk}_i, \mathsf{msk}_i)$ . Namely, the reduction  $\mathcal{B}_2$  samples  $(\mathsf{mpk}_\theta, \mathsf{msk}_\theta) \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathsf{Setup}'(\mathcal{F}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d,B),\mathcal{P}})$  for all  $\theta \neq i$  and  $\vec{u}_i \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^d$  for  $i \in [n]$ , and it uses these values to simulate mpk and answer the queries  $(\theta, \vec{x}_\theta^{j,0}, \vec{x}_\theta^{j,1})$  for all  $\theta \neq i$ .  $\mathcal{B}_2$  can also answer queries to OCorrupt, since it knows  $\mathsf{ek}_i := \vec{u}_i$  for all  $i \in [n]$ . To answer  $\mathcal{A}$ 's queries to OKeyGen, it uses its own oracle to compute the  $\mathsf{sk}_i'$  component of the secret key and its knowledge of  $\mathsf{msk}_\theta$  for all  $\theta \neq i$  and  $\vec{u}_i$  for  $i \in [n]$  to compute the remaining components, and forwards the answer to  $\mathcal{A}$ . Upon receiving the queries  $(i, \vec{x}_i^{j,0}, \vec{x}_i^{j,1})$ ,  $\mathcal{B}_2$  sends the queries  $(\vec{x}_i^{j,0} + \vec{x}_i^{1,1} - \vec{x}_i^{1,0} + \vec{u}_i \bmod p, \vec{x}_i^{j,1} + \vec{u}_i \bmod p)$  to its own experiment. The left challenge corresponds to the game  $\mathsf{G}_2$ , whereas the right challenge corresponds to the game  $\mathsf{G}_3$ . To use the AD-IND security of  $\mathcal{FE}'$ , we rely on the fact that

<span id="page-37-2"></span>
$$(\vec{x}_i^{j,0} - \vec{x}_i^{1,0})^\top \vec{y}_i = (\vec{x}_i^{j,1} - \vec{x}_i^{1,1})^\top \vec{y}_i.$$
(1)

This is required by definition of the security game, since this information is given by the ideal functionality (correctness of the scheme). For all  $i \in [n]$ , any vector  $\vec{w_i} \in [-B, 2B]^d$ , with probability at least  $1 - \frac{3dB}{p}$  over the choice of  $\vec{u_i} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^d$ , we have  $(\vec{u_i} + \vec{w_i}) \mod p = (\vec{u_i} \mod p) + \vec{w_i}$ . Together with (1), this implies that:  $(\vec{u_i} + \vec{x_i^{j,0}} + \vec{x_i^{1,1}} - \vec{x_i^{1,0}}) \mod p = (\vec{u_i} \mod p) \oplus \vec{v_i} = \vec{x_i^{j,1}} \oplus \vec{v_i} = (\vec{x_i^{j,0}} + \vec{x_i^{1,1}} - \vec{x_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i} = (\vec{x_i^{j,0}} + \vec{x_i^{1,1}} - \vec{x_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i} = (\vec{x_i^{j,0}} + \vec{x_i^{1,1}} - \vec{x_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i} = (\vec{x_i^{j,0}} + \vec{v_i^{1,1}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i} = (\vec{v_i^{j,0}} + \vec{v_i^{1,1}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i} = (\vec{v_i^{j,0}} + \vec{v_i^{1,1}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i} = (\vec{v_i^{j,0}} + \vec{v_i^{1,1}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i} = (\vec{v_i^{j,0}} + \vec{v_i^{1,1}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i} = (\vec{v_i^{j,0}} + \vec{v_i^{1,1}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{j,0}} + \vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} + \vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} + \vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} + \vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} + \vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec{v_i^{1,0}} = (\vec{v_i^{1,0}} - \vec{v_i^{1,0}}) \oplus \vec$ 

```
 \begin{array}{l} \boxed{ G_1, \boxed{G_2}, \boxed{G_3} } \vdots \\ (\mathsf{mpk}, \mathsf{msk}, (\mathsf{ek}_1, \ldots, \mathsf{ek}_n)) \leftarrow \mathsf{Setup}(1^\lambda, \mathcal{F}^{\mathsf{multi}}_{\mathsf{ipfe}(d,B),\mathsf{P}_1, \ldots, \mathsf{P}_n}) \\ \beta' \leftarrow \mathcal{A}^{\mathsf{OCorrupt}(\cdot), \mathsf{OKeyGen}(\cdot, \cdot), \mathsf{OEnc}(\cdot, \cdot, \cdot)}(\mathsf{mpk}) \\ \\ \frac{\mathsf{OCorrupt}(i) \colon}{\mathsf{Return} \ \mathsf{ek}_i .} \\ \\ \frac{\mathsf{OEnc}(i, \vec{x}_i^{j,0}, \vec{x}_i^{j,1}) \colon}{\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{ek}_i, \vec{x}_i^{j,0} + \boxed{\vec{x}_i^{1,1} - \vec{x}_i^{1,0}}) \boxed{\mathsf{ct}_i \leftarrow \mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{ek}_i, \vec{x}_i^{j,1})} \\ \mathsf{Return} \ \mathsf{ct}_i . \\ \\ \frac{\mathsf{OKeyGen}(\mathcal{S}, (\mathsf{att}_j, \vec{y}_j)_{j \in \mathcal{S}}) \colon}{\mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} \leftarrow \mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{msk}, \mathcal{S}, (\mathsf{att}_j, \vec{y}_j)_{j \in \mathcal{S}}) .} \\ \mathsf{Return} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} \leftarrow \mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{msk}, \mathcal{S}, (\mathsf{att}_j, \vec{y}_j)_{j \in \mathcal{S}}) .} \\ \mathsf{Return} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} \leftarrow \mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{msk}, \mathcal{S}, (\mathsf{att}_j, \vec{y}_j)_{j \in \mathcal{S}}) . \\ \mathsf{Return} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} \leftarrow \mathsf{KeyGen}(\mathsf{msk}, \mathcal{S}, (\mathsf{att}_j, \vec{y}_j)_{j \in \mathcal{S}}) . \\ \mathsf{Return} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} \leftarrow \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} \leftarrow \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{att}, \vec{y}} = \mathsf{Neturn} \ \mathsf{sk}
```

```
\underline{\mathsf{Setup}}(1^\lambda,\mathcal{X},\mathcal{Y}) \colon
                                                                                                                                                                                                 |\mathsf{Enc}(\mathsf{mpk}, \vec{x})|:
\mathbf{A} \leftarrow_{\mathtt{R}} \mathbb{Z}_q^{m \times n}
                                                                                                                                                                                                 \vec{s} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_q^n
                                                                                                                                                                                                  \vec{e}_1 \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbf{Z}^m,\sigma}
\mathbf{Z} \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} D_{\mathbb{Z}^{\ell \times m}, \rho} \\ \mathbf{D} \leftarrow \mathbf{Z} \mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{\ell \times n}
                                                                                                                                                                                                 \vec{e}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbf{Z}^\ell,\sigma}
                                                                                                                                                                                                 \mathsf{ct}_1 \leftarrow \mathbf{A} \vec{s} + \vec{e}_1
\mathsf{mpk} \leftarrow (\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{D})
                                                                                                                                                                                                 \left| \mathsf{ct}_2 = \mathbf{D}\vec{s} + \vec{e}_2 + \left| \frac{q}{K} \right| \cdot \vec{x} \right| 
\mathsf{msk} \leftarrow \mathbf{Z}
Return (mpk, msk)
                                                                                                                                                                                                  Return (ct_1, ct_2)
KeyGen(msk, \vec{y}):
                                                                                                                                                                                                 \mathsf{Dec}(\mathsf{ct}_1,\mathsf{ct}_2,\mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{id},\vec{y}},\vec{y}):
                                                                                                                                                                                                \overline{\mu = \vec{y}^{\scriptscriptstyle \textsf{\tiny Ct}} \cdot \mathsf{ct}_2 - \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{id},\vec{y}} \cdot \mathsf{ct}_1}
Return (\vec{y}, \mathsf{sk}_{\mathsf{id}, \vec{y}} := (\vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{Z}))
                                                                                                                                                                                                 \mu' = \arg\min_{\mu' \in \{0...K+1\}} \left| \left| \frac{q}{K} \right| \cdot \mu - \mu' \right|
                                                                                                                                                                                                 Return \mu'
```

<span id="page-42-2"></span>**Fig. 15.** The ALS inner-product functional encryption scheme  $\mathcal{FE}$  from [ALS16].

#### <span id="page-42-0"></span>The ALS inner-product functional encryption scheme A

In this appendix, we recall the inner-product functional encryption scheme from [ALS16], which we denote as ALS. The proof described here is the same as [ALS16], except for a small modification described in [WFL19]. The novelty of [WFL19] is that they described how a rerandomization technique due to [KY16] can be used to simplify the proof of the ALS scheme and improve its parameters. What is changed is that the matrix **Z** has a different distribution from [ALS16], which allows us to combine this scheme with the random-oracle based IBE of [GPV08] and the standard model IBE of [ABB10]. The construction is described in Fig. 15. This variant of ALS has the matrix  $\mathbf{Z} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} D_{\mathbb{Z}^{\ell \times m}, \rho}$ , rather than the slightly more complicated distribution in the original description of the scheme.

Choice of Parameters We first set  $\mathcal{X} = \{0, \dots, P-1\}^{\ell}$ ,  $\mathcal{Y} = \{0, \dots, V-1\}^{\ell}$ , the output must belong to gs to  $\{0,\ldots,K-1\}$  with  $K=\ell PV$ . First we set the parameters for correctness:

- From Lemma 4.2, we know that every entry of **D** is with overwhelming probability bounded by  $\omega(\log n)$ , so  $\|\mathbf{D}\| \leq \sqrt{\ell \cdot \omega(\log n)}$ , as long as  $\rho \geq \omega(\sqrt{\log n})$ .
- Now we bound  $\|\vec{y}^{\mathsf{T}} \mathbf{D} \vec{e}_1\| \leq \ell \sqrt{\ell} V \omega(\log^2 n)$  and  $\|\vec{y}\vec{e}_2\| \leq \ell V \omega(\sqrt{\log n})$ , as long as  $\sigma \geq \omega(\sqrt{\log n})$ . For decryption to suceed, we want that the error terms are smaller than  $\frac{q}{2K}$ , which implies:  $q \geq 2K\ell\sqrt{\ell}V\omega(\log^2 n)$

We therefore have the following lemma:

**Lemma A.1.** For 
$$q \ge 2K\ell\sqrt{\ell}V\omega(\log^2 n)$$
,  $\sigma, \rho > \omega(\sqrt{\log n})$  the ALS scheme from Fig. 15 is correct.

The security reduction is with respect to LWE $_{q,\alpha,n}$ , where the modulus will be much larger than K. Denote the standard deviation  $r := \alpha q$ . To ensure security, we need to satisfy the following requirements:

- NoiseGen rerandomization: looking ahead in the security proof, we require that the singular value  $s_1(\mathbf{V})$  of  $\mathbf{V}$  is bounded by  $h := \Omega(\rho(\sqrt{\ell} + \sqrt{m} + \sqrt{n}))$  (see the security proof for exact values and security parameter n). Then, in order for NoiseGen to provide security guarantees, we must set  $\sigma \geq 2 \cdot r \cdot h$ . Since h depends on the gaussian parameter  $\rho$  of matrix **Z**, this establishes a relation between  $\rho$  and  $\sigma$ .
- Min-entropy requirement: since  $\rho > \omega(\sqrt{\log n})$ , the min-entropy requirement is satisfied (as long as n > 2.303) and  $m \ge (2n \log q + 2n) / \log(4/3)$ .
- Leftover hash lemma: this is satisfied as long as m is much larger than n (see [ALS16, Lemma 11]). For the purposes of this paper, we will choose  $m = 2n \log q$ .

Putting all requirements together, we choose as parameters:

$$q \ge 2K\ell\sqrt{\ell}V\omega(\log^2 n)$$

$$\sigma = 2C\alpha q(\sqrt{m} + \sqrt{n} + \sqrt{\ell})$$

$$\rho \ge \omega(\sqrt{\log n}).$$

$$m = 2n\log q$$

{43}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-43-0"></span>LWE<sub> $q,\alpha,n$ </sub> is reducible to lattice problems in dimension n when  $q\alpha \geq \Omega(\sqrt{n})$ . With this choice of parameters, we have the following theorem:

**Theorem A.2.** [ALS16, WFL19] Let n be the security parameter,  $m \geq 2n \log q$  and  $q, \sigma, \rho, \alpha \leq \frac{\sigma}{2C\alpha q(\sqrt{m}+\sqrt{n}+\sqrt{\ell})}$  as described above. Then, the scheme from Fig. 15 is AD-IND-secure, assuming that LWE $_{q,\alpha,n}$  is hard.

*Proof.* As mentioned previously, we recall the proof of the ALS scheme from [ALS16] in which Game 2 is modified with the idea described in [WFL19].

## Game 1 This is the AD-IND game.

Game 2 Draw an error  $\vec{e} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^m,r}$  and then compute  $\vec{c} = \mathbf{A}\vec{s} + \vec{e}$ . Let  $\mathbf{I}_m$  denote the  $m \times m$  identity matrix. Now we apply Lemma 4.7 for  $\mathbf{V} = \left(\frac{\mathbf{I}_m}{\mathbf{Z}}\right) \in \mathbb{Z}^{(m+\ell)\times m}$ . Let  $\sigma' > s_1(\mathbf{V})$ . By Lemma 4.1, we know that  $s_1(\mathbf{Z}) \leq C\rho(\sqrt{\ell} + \sqrt{m} + \sqrt{\lambda})$  except with  $\frac{1}{e^{2\pi\lambda}}$  probability and we use this to argue that  $s_1(\mathbf{V}) \leq \sqrt{(C\rho(\sqrt{\ell} + \sqrt{m} + \sqrt{\lambda}))^2 + 1}$ 

By computing  $\vec{c}' = \mathbf{V}\vec{c} + \mathsf{NoiseGen}(\mathbf{V}, \sigma')$ , we obtain  $\vec{c}' = \mathbf{V} \cdot \vec{c} + \vec{f}$ , where  $\vec{f} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^{m+\ell}, 2r\sigma'}$ . Notice that we can split  $\vec{c}'$  into  $\mathsf{ct}_1 \leftarrow \mathbf{A}\vec{s} + \vec{e}_1$  and  $\mathsf{ct}_2 \leftarrow \mathbf{D}\vec{s} + \vec{e}_2$ , where  $e_1 \leftarrow \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^m, 2r\sigma'}$  and  $e_2 \leftarrow \mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^\ell, 2r\sigma'}$ . Now, to simulate  $\mathsf{ct}_2$  correctly, we need to compute  $\mathsf{ct}_2' = \mathsf{ct}_2 + \left| \frac{q}{K} \right| \cdot \vec{x}_\beta$ .

**Game 3** By the LWE assumption, we change  $\vec{c} = \mathbf{A}\vec{s} + \vec{e}$  into a uniformly random element  $\vec{u}$  of  $\mathbb{Z}_q^m$ . Now we argue that in this game, the probability the adversary has of winning is  $\frac{1}{2}$ . This step of the proof is identical to [ALS16, Theorem 2], which we recall here for completeness.

Consider  $\vec{y}^i$  the decryption key queries made by the adversary. Then,  $\langle \vec{x}_0, \vec{y}^i \rangle = \langle \vec{x}_1, \vec{y}^i \rangle$  over  $\mathbb{Z}$ , for all i. Define  $\vec{x} := \frac{1}{g}(\vec{x}_1 - \vec{x}_0) = \frac{1}{g}(x_1, \dots, x_\ell)$ , where  $g \neq 0$  is the gcd of all coefficients of  $\vec{x}_0 - \vec{x}_1$ . Since  $\langle \vec{x}, \vec{y}^i \rangle = 0$ , this means all decryption key queries must belong to the lattice  $\{\vec{y} \in \mathbb{Z}^\ell : \langle \vec{x}, \vec{y} \rangle = 0\}$ . Assume without loss of generality that the first  $n_0$  entries of  $\vec{x}$  are zero, while the rest are non-zero.

Define matrix 
$$\mathbf{Y}_{top} = \begin{pmatrix} \mathbf{I}_{n_0} & & & \\ & -x_{n_0+2} & -x_{n_0+1} & & \\ & & -x_{n_0+3} & & \\ & & & \ddots & \ddots & \\ & & & -x_\ell \ x_{\ell-1} \end{pmatrix} \in \mathbb{Z}^{\ell-1\times(\ell)}$$

Then they write  $\mathbf{Y}_{bot} = \vec{x}^{\scriptscriptstyle \top} \in \mathbb{Z}^{1 \times \ell}$  and define  $\mathbf{Y} := \left(\frac{\mathbf{Y}_{top}}{\mathbf{Y}_{bot}}\right) \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{\ell \times \ell}$ . By induction, they show that  $\det(\mathbf{Y}\mathbf{Y}^{\scriptscriptstyle \top}) = (\prod_{k=n_0+2}^{\ell-1} x_k^2) \cdot \|\vec{x}\|^4$ . As long as q is large enough, this quantity is non-zero modulo q, which implies that  $\mathbf{Y}$  is invertible modulo q. Observe that for  $(\vec{g}_1, \vec{g}_2) = \operatorname{NoiseGen}(\mathbf{V}, \sigma') : \operatorname{ct}_1 = \vec{u} + \vec{g}_1$  and  $\operatorname{ct}_2 = \mathbf{Z} \cdot \vec{u} + \vec{g}_2 + \left\lfloor \frac{q}{K} \right\rfloor \cdot \vec{x}_\beta$ , where  $\vec{u} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_q^m$ .

The proof strategy of [ALS16] is to show that the distribution of  $\mathbf{Y} \cdot \mathsf{ct}_2 \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{\ell \times 1}$  is almost independent of  $\beta$  and therefore the probability of winning the game is almost  $\frac{1}{2}$ .

Rewrite 
$$\operatorname{ct}_2 = \mathbf{Y}^{-1} \cdot \mathbf{Y} \cdot (\mathbf{Z}\vec{u} + \vec{g}_2 + \left| \frac{q}{K} \right| \cdot \vec{x}_\beta) \bmod q$$
.

Notice that  $\mathbf{Y}\mathsf{ct}_2 = \left(\frac{\mathbf{Y}_{top}\mathsf{ct}_2}{\mathbf{Y}_{bot}\mathsf{ct}_2}\right)$ . Now  $\mathbf{Y}_{top}\mathsf{ct}_2$  does not depend on  $\beta$  because  $\mathbf{Y}_{top} \cdot \vec{x}_0 = \mathbf{Y}_{top} \cdot \vec{x}_1$ , from the functional encryption constraints.

It remains to analyze  $Y_{bot}$ ct<sub>2</sub>. Here, [ALS16] prove the following variant of the leftover hash lemma:

**Lemma A.3.** [ALS16] Conditioned on  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{Z}\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{Y}_{top}, \mathbf{Y}_{top}\mathbf{Z})$ , the min-entropy of  $\mathbf{Y}_{bot}Z$  is greater or equal than  $n \log q + 2\lambda$ .

{44}------------------------------------------------

<span id="page-44-1"></span>This lemma can be applied as long as the following lemma due to [PR06] and adapted in [ALS16] holds for the standard deviation used to generate **Z**:

**Lemma A.4.** [ALS16] Let  $\Lambda = k \cdot \mathbb{Z}$  be a 1-dimensional lattice. For any  $\sigma \geq 10 \cdot k, b \in \lambda$  and  $c \in \mathbb{R}$ , we have that  $\mathcal{D}_{\Lambda,\sigma,c}(b) \leq 3/4$ . In particular, we have  $H_{\infty}(D_{\Lambda,\sigma,c}) \geq 0.4$ , where  $H_{\infty}()$  denotes the min-entropy.

As long as the previous two lemmas hold, one can use the leftover hash lemma for the seed  $\vec{u}$  and randomness  $\mathbf{Y}_{bot}\mathbf{Z}$  to argue that given  $(\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{Z}\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{Y}_{top}, \mathbf{Y}_{top}\mathbf{Z})$ , the pair  $(\vec{u}, \mathbf{Y}_{bot}\mathbf{Z}\vec{u})$  is  $1/2^{\lambda}$  statistically close from the uniform distribution over  $\mathbb{Z}_q^m \times \mathbb{Z}_q$ , therefore hiding  $\beta$ . The variant of the leftover hash lemma used is the following:

**Lemma A.5** (Lemma 10 in [ALS16]). Let  $n, m, q \geq 2$  be positive integers. Assume that  $q = p^k$  for p prime and  $k \geq 1$ . Assume further that  $m \geq 2n \log_2 q$ . Let  $\sigma \geq \Omega(\sqrt{n + \log m})$  and  $c \in \mathbb{Z}^m$ . Then for  $\mathbf{A} \in \mathbb{Z}_q^{m \times n}$  sampled uniformly and  $\vec{e} \in \mathbb{Z}^m$  sampled from  $\mathcal{D}_{\mathbb{Z}^m,\sigma,c}$ , the distribution of the pair  $(\mathbf{A}, \vec{e}^\mathsf{T} \cdot \mathbf{A})$  is within statistical distance  $2^{-\Omega(n)}$  of uniform over  $\mathbb{Z}_q^{m \times n} \times \mathbb{Z}_q^n$ .

#### <span id="page-44-0"></span>**Instantiations of Predicate Encodings** В

#### **B.1** Inner-product encryption with short ciphertexts [BBG05]

The predicate is described by a vector  $\vec{y} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$ , takes as input an attribute  $\vec{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$ , and outputs 1 if  $\vec{x}^\top \vec{y} = 0$ , 0 otherwise.

- Param: returns the parameters  $(n+2,|\mathsf{ct}|=1,|\mathsf{sk}|=n+1).$
- EncCt $(\vec{x})$ : given  $\vec{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  returns a matrix  $\mathbf{C} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+2) \times 1}$  such that  $\mathbf{C}^{\top}(v_1, v_2, \vec{w}) = v_1 + \vec{x}^{\top} \vec{w} \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ . EncKey $(\vec{y})$ : given  $\vec{y} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  returns a matrix  $\mathbf{K} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+3) \times (n+1)}$  such that  $\mathbf{K}^{\top}(\alpha, v_1, v_2, \vec{w}) = (v_2 \vec{y} + \vec{w}, v_1 + \alpha) \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  $\mathbb{Z}_p^{n+1}$ .
- $\mathsf{Decode}(\vec{x}, \vec{y})$ : if  $\vec{x}^\top \vec{y} = 0$ , it returns the vector  $\vec{d} := \begin{pmatrix} -1 \\ \vec{x} \\ 1 \end{pmatrix} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n+2}$ .

#### **B.2** Ciphertext-policy ABE for monotone span programs [GPSW06]

We recall the definition of read-once monotone span programs [KW93]. The predicate is described by a matrix  $\mathbf{M} \in$  $\mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell \times n}$ , takes as input an attribute  $\vec{x} = (x_1, \dots, x_n) \in \{0, 1\}^n$ , and outputs 1 if the vector  $(1, 0, \dots, 0) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  is in the column span of  $\mathbf{M}_{\vec{x}}$ , which denotes the collection of vectors  $\{\mathbf{M}_j: x_j=1\}$  where  $\mathbf{M}_j$  denotes the j'th column of **M**. That is,  $\vec{x}$  satisfies **M** if there exists constants  $\omega_1, \ldots, \omega_n \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  such that

$$\sum_{j:x_j=1} \omega_j \mathbf{M}_j = (1, 0, \dots, 0)$$
(2)

Observe that the constants  $\{\omega_j\}_{j\in[n]}$  can be computed in time polynomial in the size of the matrix M via Gaussian elimination.

- Param: returns the parameters  $(n+\ell,|\mathsf{ct}|=n,|\mathsf{sk}|=n+1)$ . EncCt( $\mathbf{M}$ ): given  $\mathbf{M} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n \times \ell}$  returns a matrix  $\mathbf{C} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+2) \times 1}$  such that  $\mathbf{C}^{\top}(\vec{w},\vec{u},\gamma) = (w_1 + (\gamma,\vec{u})^{\top}\mathbf{M}_1,\ldots,$  $w_n + (\gamma, \vec{u})^{\top} \mathbf{M}_n) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$ , where  $\mathbf{M}_j$  denotes the j'th column of  $\mathbf{M}$ .
- EncKey $(\vec{x})$ : given  $\vec{x} \in \{0,1\}^n$ , returns a matrix  $\mathbf{K} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+3)\times(n+1)}$  such that  $\mathbf{K}^{\top}(\alpha, \vec{w}, \vec{u}, \gamma) = (\gamma + \alpha, x_1 w_1, \dots, x_n w_n) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(n+3)\times(n+1)}$  $\mathbb{Z}_p^{n+1}$ .
- Decode $(\vec{x}, \vec{y})$ : if  $\vec{x}^{\top} \vec{y} = 0$ , it returns the vector  $\vec{d} := (x_1 \omega_1, \dots, x_n \omega_n, 1, \omega_1, \dots, \omega_n) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{2n+1}$ .

More predicate encodings can be found in [CGW15], yielding inner-product encryption with short keys [BB04], non-zero inner-product encryption (introduced in [AL10]), spatial encryption [BH08, BBG05, LW10], ciphertextpolicy for arithmetic span programs [CGW15].

{45}------------------------------------------------

#### <span id="page-45-0"></span>**Instantiations of Function Encodings** C

### **C.1** Identity-Based Inner-Product Functional Encryption

Each function is described by an identity id  $\in \mathbb{Z}_p$  and a vector  $\vec{y} \in [0, B]^d$ , takes as input another identity id  $' \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ and a vector  $\vec{x} \in [0, B]^d$ , and outputs  $\vec{x}^{\top} \vec{y}$  if id = id', 0 otherwise. The partial information part $(\vec{x}, id) = id$ .

- Param: returns the parameters (2d, |ct| = d, |sk| = n + 1).
- $\operatorname{EncCt}(\vec{x},\operatorname{id})$ : given  $\vec{x}\in\mathbb{Z}_p^n$  and  $\operatorname{id}\in\mathbb{Z}_p$ , returns a matrix  $\mathbf{C}\in\mathbb{Z}_p^{(2d+1)\times d}$  such that  $\mathbf{C}^{\top}(w_0,\vec{w}_1,\vec{w}_2)=(w_0\vec{x}+1)$  $\vec{w}_1 + \mathsf{id}\vec{w}_2) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^d$ .
- EncKey $(\vec{y}, \mathsf{id}')$ : given  $\vec{y} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$  and  $\mathsf{id}' \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ , returns a matrix  $\mathbf{K} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(2d+1) \times 1}$  such that  $\mathbf{K}^\top(w_0, \vec{w}_1, \vec{w}_2) =$  $\vec{y}^{\top}(\vec{w}_1 + \mathsf{id}'\vec{w}_2) \in \mathbb{Z}_p.$
- Decode(id, id',  $\vec{y}$ ): if  $\vec{x}^{\top}\vec{y} = 0$ , it returns the vector  $\vec{d} := (\vec{y}, -1) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{d+1}$ .

Correctness. Let  $id \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ ,  $\vec{x}, \vec{y} \in [0, B]^d$ ,  $\mathbf{C} := \mathsf{EncCt}(\vec{x}, \mathsf{id})$ ,  $\mathbf{K} := \mathsf{EncKey}(\vec{y}, \mathsf{id})$  and  $\vec{d} := \mathsf{Decode}(\mathsf{id}, \mathsf{id}', \vec{y})$ . We have  $(w_0, \vec{w}_1, \vec{w}_2)^{\top} (\mathbf{C} | \mathbf{K}) \vec{d} = (w_0 \vec{x} + \vec{w}_1 + \mathrm{id} \vec{w}_2)^{\top} \vec{y} - \vec{y}^{\top} (\vec{w}_1 + \mathrm{id} \vec{w}_2) = w_0 \vec{x}^{\top} \vec{y}$ .

Security. Let  $\mathrm{id}^0 = \mathrm{id}^1$ ,  $\mathrm{id}' \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ ,  $\vec{x}^0$ ,  $\vec{x}^1$ ,  $\vec{y} \in [0,B]^d$  for all  $b \in \{0,1\}$ ,  $\mathbf{C}^b := \mathrm{EncCt}(\vec{x}^b,\mathrm{id}^b)$ ,  $\mathbf{K} := \mathrm{EncKey}(\vec{y},\mathrm{id})$ . If  $\mathrm{id}^0 = \mathrm{id}^1 = \mathrm{id}'$ , then it must be that  $\vec{y}^\top \vec{x}^0 = \vec{y}^\top \vec{x}^1$ . In that case, security relies on the fact that the following are identified. cally distributed:  $\vec{w}_1$  and  $\vec{w}_1 + \vec{x}^1 - \vec{x}^0$ , for  $\vec{w}_1 \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^d$ . The left most distribution corresponds to  $(w_0 | \vec{w}_1^\top | \vec{w}_2^\top)(\mathbf{C}^0 | \mathbf{K})$ whereas the rightmost distribution corresponds to  $(w_0|\vec{w}_1^{\top}|\vec{w}_2^{\top})(\mathbf{C}^1|\mathbf{K})$ .

If  $id^0 = id^1 \neq id'$ , the security relies on the fact that the following are identically distributed:  $(\vec{w}_1 + id\vec{w}_2, \vec{w}_1 +$  $\mathsf{id}'\vec{w}_2)$  and  $(\vec{w}_1 + \mathsf{id}\vec{w}_2 + \vec{x}^1 - \vec{x}^0, \vec{w}_1 + \mathsf{id}'\vec{w}_2)$ , where  $\vec{w}_1, \vec{w}_2 \leftarrow_{\mathsf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^d$ . The left most distribution corresponds to  $(w_0|\vec{w}_1^{\top}|\vec{w}_2^{\top})(\mathbf{C}^0|\mathbf{K})$  whereas the rightmost distribution corresponds to  $(w_0|\vec{w}_1^{\top}|\vec{w}_2^{\top})(\mathbf{C}^1|\mathbf{K})$ .

#### C.2 Orthogonality Testing with Inner-Product Functional Encryption

Each function is described by a predicate vector  $\tilde{\vec{y}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^\ell$  and a message vector  $\vec{y} \in [0,B]^d$ , takes as input a pair  $(\widetilde{\vec{x}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell} \setminus \{\mathbf{0}\}, \vec{x} \in [0, B]^d)$ , and returns  $\vec{x}^{\top} \vec{y}$  if  $\widetilde{\vec{x}}^{\top} \widetilde{\vec{y}} = 0$ . The partial information is  $\mathsf{part}(\widetilde{\vec{x}}, \vec{x}) = \widetilde{\vec{x}}$ .

- Param: returns the parameters  $(d\ell+1,|\mathsf{ct}|=1,|\mathsf{sk}|=n+1)$ .  $\mathsf{EncCt}(\vec{x},\widetilde{\vec{x}})$ : returns a matrix  $\mathbf{C} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(d\ell+2) \times d}$  such that  $\mathbf{C}^\top(w_0,u,\vec{w}_1,\ldots,\vec{w}_\ell) = \mathbf{W}\widetilde{\vec{x}} + w_0\vec{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^d$ , where  $\mathbf{W} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{d \times \ell}$  denotes the matrix whose i'th column is  $\vec{w_i}$ , for all  $i \in [\ell]$ .
- $\ \operatorname{EncKey}(\vec{y}, \widetilde{\vec{y}}) : \text{returns a matrix } \mathbf{K} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(d\ell+2) \times \ell} \text{ such that } \mathbf{K}^\top(w_0, u, \vec{w}_1, \dots, \vec{w}_\ell) = \mathbf{W}^\top \vec{y} + u \widetilde{\vec{y}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^\ell.$
- Decode $(\vec{x}, \vec{y})$ : if  $\vec{x}^{\top} \vec{y} = 0$ , it returns the vector  $\vec{d} := (\vec{y}, \tilde{\vec{x}}) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{d+\ell}$ .

Correctness. Let  $\widetilde{\vec{x}},\widetilde{\vec{y}}\in\mathbb{Z}_p^\ell$  such that  $\widetilde{\vec{x}}^{\top}\widetilde{\vec{y}}=0,\,\vec{x},\vec{y}\in[0,B]^d,\,\mathbf{C}:=\mathsf{EncCt}(\vec{x},\widetilde{\vec{x}}),\,\mathbf{K}:=\mathsf{EncKey}(\vec{y},\widetilde{\vec{y}})$  and  $\vec{d} := \mathsf{Decode}(\widetilde{\vec{x}}, \widetilde{\vec{y}}, \vec{y}). \text{ We have } (w_0, u, \vec{w_1}, \dots, \vec{w_\ell})^\top (\mathbf{C} | \mathbf{K}) \vec{d} = \vec{y}^\top (\mathbf{W} \widetilde{\vec{x}} + w_0 \vec{x}) - (\vec{y}^\top \mathbf{W} + u \widetilde{\vec{y}}) \widetilde{\vec{x}} = w_0 \vec{x}^\top \vec{y},$ where the last equality uses the fact that  $\tilde{\vec{x}}^{\top}\tilde{\vec{y}}=0$ .

 $\textit{Security.} \ \ \operatorname{Let} \ \widetilde{\vec{x}}^0 = \widetilde{\vec{x}}^1, \widetilde{\vec{y}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^\ell, \vec{x}^0, \vec{x}^1, \vec{y} \in [0, B]^d, \ \text{for all} \ b \in \{0, 1\}, \ \mathbf{C}^b := \operatorname{EncCt}(\vec{x}^b, \widetilde{\vec{x}}^b), \ \mathbf{K} := \operatorname{EncKey}(\vec{y}, \widetilde{\vec{y}}). \ \text{If} \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}^b = (0, 1), \ \vec{x}$  $\widetilde{\vec{y}}^{\top}\widetilde{\vec{x}}^{0} = \widetilde{\vec{y}}^{\top}\widetilde{\vec{x}}^{1} = 0$ , then it must be that  $\vec{y}^{\top}\vec{x}^{0} = \vec{y}^{\top}\vec{x}^{1}$ . In that case, security relies on the fact that the following are identically distributed:  $\mathbf{W}$  and  $\mathbf{W} + w_{0}(\vec{x}^{1} - \vec{x}^{0})\vec{v}^{\top}$ , where  $\mathbf{W} \leftarrow_{\mathbf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{d \times \ell}$ , and  $\vec{v} \in \mathbb{Z}_{p}^{\ell}$  is an arbitrary vector such that

 $\widetilde{\vec{x}}^{\top} \vec{v} = 1$  (such a vector exists since  $\widetilde{\vec{x}} \neq \mathbf{0}$ ).

If  $\widetilde{\vec{y}}^{\top} \widetilde{\vec{x}}^{0} = \widetilde{\vec{y}}^{\top} \widetilde{\vec{x}}^{1} \neq 0$ , then security relies on the fact that the following are identically distributed:  $(\mathbf{W}, u)$  and  $\left(\mathbf{W} + \frac{w_0(\vec{x}^1 - \vec{x}^0)\widetilde{\vec{y}}^\top}{\widetilde{\vec{y}}^\top\widetilde{\vec{x}}^0}, u - \frac{w_0(\vec{x}^1 - \vec{x}^0)^\top\vec{y}}{\widetilde{\vec{y}}^\top\widetilde{\vec{x}}^0}\right)$ , with  $\mathbf{W} \leftarrow_{\mathbf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{d \times \ell}$  and  $u \leftarrow_{\mathbf{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p$ . The left most distribution corresponds to  $(w_0, u, \vec{w}_1, \dots, \vec{w}_\ell)^\top (\mathbf{C}^0 | \mathbf{K})$  whereas the rightmost distribution corresponds to  $(w_0, u, \vec{w}_1, \dots, \vec{w}_\ell)^\top (\mathbf{C}^1 | \mathbf{K})$ .

{46}------------------------------------------------

## C.3 Fully-hiding Orthogonality Testing with Inner-Product Functional Encryption

Each function is described by a predicate vector  $\widetilde{\underline{y}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^\ell$  and a message vector  $\overrightarrow{y} \in [0,B]^d$ , takes as input a pair  $\left(\widetilde{\vec{x}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{\ell} \setminus \{\mathbf{0}\}, \vec{x} \in [0,B]^d\right)$ , and returns  $\vec{x}^{\top} \vec{y}$  if  $\widetilde{\vec{x}}^{\top} \widetilde{\vec{y}} = 0$ . There is no partial information:  $\mathsf{part}(\widetilde{\vec{x}}, \vec{x}) = \emptyset$ .

- Param: returns the parameters  $(2d+d\ell,|\mathsf{ct}|=1,|\mathsf{sk}|=n+1)$ .  $\mathsf{EncCt}(\vec{x},\widetilde{\vec{x}})$ : returns a matrix  $\mathbf{C} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(2d+d\ell+1)\times(d\ell+d)}$  such that  $\mathbf{C}^\top(w_0,\vec{u},\vec{v},\mathsf{vect}(\mathbf{W})) = (\mathsf{vect}(\vec{u}\widetilde{\vec{x}}^\top+\mathbf{W}),\vec{u}+w_0\vec{x}) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^d$ , where for any matrix  $\mathbf{M} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n\times m}$ , we denote by  $\mathsf{vect}(\mathbf{M}) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{nm}$  the vector such that for all  $\vec{x} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^n$ ,  $\vec{y} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^m$ ,  $\operatorname{vect}(\mathbf{M})^{\top}(\vec{x} \otimes \vec{y}) = \vec{x}^{\top} \mathbf{M} \vec{y}$ . Here,  $\vec{u}, \vec{v} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^d$ , and  $\mathbf{W} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^{d \times \ell}$ .

  -  $\operatorname{EncKey}(\vec{y}, \widetilde{\vec{y}})$ : returns a matrix  $\mathbf{K} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{(2d+d\ell+1) \times 1}$  such that  $\mathbf{K}^{\top}(w_0, \vec{u}, \vec{v}, \operatorname{vect}(\mathbf{W})) = \vec{y}^{\top} \mathbf{W} \widetilde{\vec{y}} + \vec{y}^{\top} \vec{v} \in \mathbb{Z}_p$ .

  -  $\operatorname{Decode}(\widetilde{\vec{y}}, \vec{y})$ : it returns the vector  $\vec{d} := (\vec{y} \otimes \widetilde{\vec{y}}, \vec{y}, -1) \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{d\ell+d+1}$ .

Correctness. Let  $\widetilde{\vec{x}}, \widetilde{\vec{y}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^\ell$  such that  $\widetilde{\vec{x}}^\top \widetilde{\vec{y}} = 0, \vec{x}, \vec{y} \in [0, B]^d, \mathbf{C} := \mathsf{EncCt}(\vec{x}, \widetilde{\vec{x}}), \mathbf{K} := \mathsf{EncKey}(\vec{y}, \widetilde{\vec{y}})$  and  $\vec{d} := \mathsf{EncKey}(\vec{y}, \widetilde{\vec{y}})$  $\mathsf{Decode}(\widetilde{\vec{y}}, \vec{y}). \text{ We have } (w_0, \vec{u}, \vec{v}, \mathsf{vect}(\mathbf{W}))^\top (\mathbf{C} | \mathbf{K}) \vec{d} = \vec{y}^\top (\vec{u} \widetilde{\vec{x}}^\top + \mathbf{W}) \widetilde{\vec{y}} + (\vec{v} + w_0 \vec{x})^\top \vec{y} - \vec{y}^\top \mathbf{W} \widetilde{\vec{y}} + \vec{y}^\top \vec{v} = w_0 \vec{x}^\top \vec{y},$ where the last equality uses the fact that  $\tilde{\vec{x}}^{\top}\tilde{\vec{y}}=0$ .

Security. Let  $\widetilde{\vec{x}}^0, \widetilde{\vec{x}}^1, \widetilde{\vec{y}} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^\ell, \vec{x}^0, \vec{x}^1, \vec{y} \in [0, B]^d$ , for all  $b \in \{0, 1\}$ ,  $\mathbf{C}^b := \mathsf{EncCt}(\vec{x}^b, \widetilde{\vec{x}}^b)$ ,  $\mathbf{K} := \mathsf{EncKey}(\vec{y}, \widetilde{\vec{y}})$ . If  $\vec{y}^{\top}\vec{x}^{0} = \vec{y}^{\top}\vec{x}^{1} = 0$ , then it must be that  $\vec{y}^{\top}\vec{x}^{0} = \vec{y}^{\top}\vec{x}^{1}$ . In that case, security relies on the fact that the following are identically distributed:  $\vec{v}$  and  $\vec{v} + w_0(\vec{x}^1 - \vec{x}^0)$ , where  $\vec{v} \leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}} \mathbb{Z}_p^d$ .

If  $\tilde{\vec{y}}^{\top}\tilde{\vec{x}}^{0} \neq 0$  and  $\tilde{\vec{y}}^{\top}\tilde{\vec{x}}^{1} \neq 0$ , then we show that  $(w_{0},\vec{u},\vec{v},\text{vect}(\mathbf{W}))^{\top}(\mathbf{C}^{b}|\mathbf{K})$  is independent of b, using the fact that the following are identically distributed:  $(\mathbf{W},\vec{v})$  and  $(\mathbf{W}+\vec{u}(\vec{x}^{1}-\vec{x}^{0})^{\top},\vec{v}+w_{0}(\vec{x}^{1}-\vec{x}^{0}))$ , with  $\mathbf{W}\leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}}\mathbb{Z}_{p}^{d\times\ell}$  and  $\vec{v}\leftarrow_{\mathbb{R}}\mathbb{Z}_{p}^{d}$ . The left most distribution corresponds to  $(w_{0},u,\vec{w}_{1},\ldots,\vec{w}_{\ell})^{\top}(\mathbf{C}^{b}|\mathbf{K})^{\top}(w_{0},u,\vec{w}_{1},\ldots,\vec{w}_{\ell}):=$  $(\text{vect}(\vec{u}\vec{x}^{b^{\top}} + \mathbf{W}), \vec{v} + w_0\vec{x}^b, \vec{y}^{\top}\mathbf{W}\vec{y} + \vec{y}^{\top}\vec{v})$  whereas the rightmost distribution corresponds to  $(\text{vect}(\mathbf{W}), \vec{v}, \vec{y}^{\top}\mathbf{W}\vec{y} + \vec{y}^{\top}\vec{v})$  $\vec{y}^{\top}\vec{v} - w_0\vec{y}^{\top}\vec{x}^b - \vec{y}^{\top}\vec{u}\underbrace{\tilde{\vec{y}}^{\top}\cdot\tilde{\vec{x}}^b}_{\neq 0}$ ). If  $\vec{y} = 0$ , then the gray term in the last distribution disappears, which means the

distribution is independent of b. If  $\vec{y} \neq \mathbf{0}$ , the value  $\vec{y}^{\top}\vec{u}$  is uniformly random over  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ , and since the value  $\tilde{\vec{y}}^{\top} \cdot \tilde{\vec{x}}^b$  is different from 0, that means the gray term itself is uniformly random over  $\mathbb{Z}_p$ , independent of b.

## C.4 Ciphertext-policy ABE for Read-once Monotone Span Programs with Inner-Product Functional **Encryption**

Each function is described by a characteristic vector  $\vec{a} = (a_1, \dots, a_n) \in \{0, 1\}^n$ , and a vector  $\vec{y} \in [O, B]^d$ . It takes as input an access structure, which is a matrix  $\mathbf{M} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{n \times \ell}$ , and a vector  $\vec{x} \in [0, B]^d$  and outputs  $\vec{x}^\top \vec{y}$  if the vector  $(1,0,\ldots,0)^{\top} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{1\times \ell}$  is in the row span of  $\mathbf{M}_{\vec{a}}$ , which denotes the collection of vectors  $\{\mathbf{M}_j: a_j=1\}$  where  $\mathbf{M}_j$ denotes the j'th row of M. That is,  $\vec{a}$  satisfies M if there exists constants  $\omega_1, \ldots, \omega_n \in \mathbb{Z}_p$  such that

<span id="page-46-0"></span>
$$\sum_{j:a_j=1} \omega_j \mathbf{M}_j = (1, 0, \dots, 0)$$
(3)

Observe that the constants  $\{\omega_j\}_{j\in[n]}$  can be computed in time polynomial in the size of the matrix M via Gaussian elimination.

The partial information is:  $part(\vec{a}, \vec{x}) = \vec{a}$ .

- Param: returns the parameters  $(2d + d\ell, |ct| = 1, |sk| = n + 1)$ .
- EncCt $(\vec{x}, \mathbf{M})$ : returns a matrix  $\mathbf{C} \in \mathbb{Z}_p^{2dn \times d(n+1)}$  such that  $\mathbf{C}^{\top}(w_0, \vec{v}, \mathsf{vect}(\mathbf{U}), \vec{w}_1, \dots, \vec{w}_n) = ((\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 + (\vec{v}|\mathbf{U})\mathbf{M}_1 +
